Rulings on the Nature of Inclination and Its Coexistence
Enumerates two rulings on mayl and its relation to resistance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two key rulings on natural and forced inclinations (mayl) and how they inform the debate on whether mayl is identical to resistance or its underlying principle.

Core Idea
Inclination is not mere resistance; its essence is revealed through rulings on natural and forced motions.

The scholars of kalām debated whether mayl (inclination) is the same as the sensible resistance we feel when pushing an object, or whether it is the intrinsic principle that causes that resistance. To answer this, they examined two specific cases: the state of a body in its natural place, and the interaction of natural and forced inclinations in the same direction. These rulings shed light on the subtle nature of mayl and its effects.

Guided Path
1
Understand the central question
Read the opening sentence: are the scholars hesitant about mayl being resistance or its principle? Notice the stakes of this debate.
This question underpins the entire passage.
2
Examine the first ruling
Natural inclination does not exist in a body when it is in its natural place. Why? Because otherwise it would seek what is already attained or abandon it.
This argument only works if mayl is the principle, not the resistance itself.
3
Reflect on the implication
The author notes that this reasoning applies to the principle (mabda') because effects can be delayed or blocked.
This supports the idea that mayl is a distinct principle.
4
Examine the second ruling
Natural and forced inclinations cannot coexist toward opposite directions, but they can coexist toward the same direction, as in a stone pushed downward.
Consider the example of a stone thrown upward vs. pushed downward.
5
Connect to the central question
These rulings confirm that mayl is a real principle distinct from its effects, and explain observable phenomena like faster motion when pushed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إنهم قد ذكروا أحكاماً تدل على ترددهم في أن الميل نفس المدافعة المحسوسة، أو مبدؤها القريب الذي يوجد عند كون الحجر صاعداً (۳) الهواء، أو ساكناً على الأرض فيها أن الميل الطبيعي لا يوجد في الجسم عند كونه في حيزه الطبيعي، وإلا فإما أن يميل إليه فيلزم طلب حصول الحاصل، أو عنه فيلزم أن يكون المطلوب بالطبع متروكاً بالطبع، ولا يتأتى هذا في مبدأ الميل إذ ربما يتخلف الأثر عنه لفقد شرط، أو وجود مانع، ومنها أن الميل الطبيعي لا يجامع الميل القسري إلى جهتين، لأن امتناع المدافعة إلى جهة مع المدافعة عنها ضروري فالحجر المرمي إلى فوق لا يكون فيه مدافعة هابطة بالفعل، بل بالقوة، بمعنى أن من شأنه أن يوجد فيه ذلك عند زوال غلبة القوة القسرية وإما إلى جهة واحدة فقد يجتمعان كما في الحجر المدفوع إلى أسفل، فإن فيه مدافعة (1) هابطة يقتضيها الحجر إذا خلي وطبعه وأخرى أحدثها فيه القاسر على حسب قوته وقصده، ولهذا يكون حركته حينئذ أسرع مما إذا سقط بنفسه فهبط، وتتفاوت تلك السرعة بتفاوت قوة القاسر.
Then they have mentioned rulings that indicate their hesitation as to whether mayl is the sensed resistance itself, or its proximate principle which exists when the stone is ascending through the air, or stationary on the ground. Among them: that natural inclination does not exist in a body when it is in its natural place; otherwise it would either incline toward it, necessitating the request for the attainment of what is already attained, or away from it, necessitating that what is sought by nature is abandoned by nature. And this is not possible in the principle of inclination, because the effect may be delayed due to the lack of a condition or the presence of an obstacle. And among them: that natural inclination does not coexist with forced inclination toward two [opposite] directions, because the impossibility of resistance toward a direction together with resistance away from it is necessary. So the stone thrown upward does not have an actual downward resistance, but only potential, meaning that it is its nature to have that when the dominance of the forced force ceases. But toward one direction they may coexist, as in the stone pushed downward, for it has a downward resistance necessitated by the stone when left to its nature, and another caused by the pusher according to his force and intent; hence its motion then is faster than if it fell by itself, and that speed varies with the variation of the pusher's force.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage presents two rulings (aḥkām) that reveal the scholars' uncertainty about whether mayl is identical to sensible resistance (mudāfaʿa) or is its immediate cause. The first ruling: natural inclination does not exist in a body when it is in its natural place (e.g., a stone at rest on the ground). If it did, it would either incline toward its place (impossible, as it is already there) or away from it (impossible, as nature seeks its place). The author notes that this argument applies to the principle of mayl, not necessarily to its effect, which may be blocked. The second ruling: natural and forced inclinations cannot coexist toward two opposite directions simultaneously; a stone thrown upward has only potential downward resistance, actualized when the forced force weakens. However, they can coexist toward one direction, as in a stone pushed downward: both its natural downward tendency and the forced downward push combine, making it fall faster than if dropped naturally. The speed increases with the pusher's force.

Key Terms
مدافعة (mudāfaʿa)
مبدأ (mabda')
حيز طبيعي (ḥayz ṭabīʿī)
ميل قسري (mayl qasrī)
ميل طبيعي (mayl ṭabīʿī)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that natural inclination is completely absent in a body in its natural place.
Correction: The passage clarifies that it is not absent; rather, its effect is not realized because the goal is already attained, but the principle (mabda') may still exist.

Checkpoint

B) Natural and forced inclinations combine, making it fall faster.

D) Only forced inclination exists.

Key Takeaway
The two rulings confirm that mayl is a real principle distinct from its effects, and they explain why a pushed object moves faster than a naturally falling one.

?

The Stone Motion Argument for Natural Inclination
The author presents the stone motion argument for natural inclination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the stone's forced and natural motion demonstrates the existence of a natural inclination, challenging the philosophers' concept of nature.

Core Idea
The stone's motion reveals a natural resistance that grows as forced motion fades.

This passage presents the first of two arguments used to prove the existence of natural inclination. By analysing the stone's upward and downward motion, the philosophers attempt to show that a natural principle must be at work. We will follow their reasoning and later see how the Imam responds.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage describing the stone's motion. Notice the description of how forced motion weakens and natural motion strengthens.
Pay attention to the phrase 'mabda' al-mudāfaʿa' (principle of resistance).
2
Identify the two types of motion
The passage distinguishes between forced (qasrī) and natural (ṭabīʿī) motion. Note how their intensities change over time.
3
Understand the role of air resistance
The forced inclination weakens due to collisions with air. This allows the natural inclination to increase until it overcomes.
4
Reflect on the implication
Why does this prove natural inclination exists? Because the stone's return cannot be due to forced motion alone; something internal must take over.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها ما ذكروا في بيان سبب أن الحجر الذي يتحرك صاعداً. بالقسر، ثم ) يرجع هابطاً بالطبع، أن حركته القسرية تشتد ابتداء وتضعف عند القرب من النهاية، والطبيعية بالعكس، لأن ميله القسري يزداد ضعفاً بمصاكات تتصل عليه من مقاومة الهواء المخروق، فيزداد الميل الطبيعي أعني مبدأ المدافعة قوة إلى أن يتعادلا ثم يأخذ القسري في الانتقاص، والطبيعي في الغلبة فيأخذ حركته في الاشتداد ومنها استدلالهم على وجود الميل الطبيعي بأن الحجرين المرميين بقوة واحدة إذا اختلفا في الصغر والكبر اختلفت حركتاهما في السرعة والبطء وليس ذاك إلا لكون المقاوم الذي هو الميل الطبيعي أعني مبدأ المدافعة في الكبير أكثر منه في الصغير، لأن التقدير عدم التفاوت في الفاعل والقابل إلا بذلك . وأجاب الإمام : بأن الطبيعة قوة سارية في الجسم منقسمة بانقسامه فيكون في الكبير أكثر، وبزيادة المقاومة أجدر والفلاسفة يزعمون أنه أمر ثابت ليس مما يشتد ويضعف أو يقل ويكثر في الجسم الواحد حتى إن (۳) طبيعة كل الماء وبعضه واحد، ولا يتبين الحق من ذلك إلا بمعرفة حقيقة ما هو المراد بالطبيعة ها هنا، وهم لم يزيدوا على أن الطبيعة قد يقال لما يصدر عنه الحركة والسكون أولاً، وبالذات دون شعور وإرادة، وقد يقال لما تصدر عنه أمر لا تتخلف عنه ، ولا يفتقر الصدور إلى علة خارجة عنه كنزول الحجر إلى السفل، وقد يخص بما يصدر عنه الحركات على نهج واحد دون شعور، وقد تسمى كل قوة جسمانية طبيعة ، وشيء من ذلك لا يفيد معرفة حقيقية، وأما إطلاقها على المزاج، أو على الكيفية الغالبة ( ) من الكيفيات المتضادة، أو على الحرارة الغريزية، أو على النفس النباتية، أو نحو ذلك (ه) على ما يذكره الأطباء فمختص بالمركبات .
And among them is what they mentioned in explaining the reason that the stone which moves upward by force then returns downward by nature: that its forced motion intensifies at the beginning and weakens near the end, and the natural motion is opposite, because its forced inclination increasingly weakens due to collisions connected to it from the resistance of the air that is cut, so the natural inclination—that is, the principle of resistance—increases in strength until they become equal, then the forced begins to decrease and the natural to prevail, so its motion begins to intensify. And among them is their proof for the existence of natural inclination: that two stones thrown with equal force, if they differ in smallness and bigness, their motions differ in speed and slowness, and that is not except because the resistant—which is natural inclination, i.e., the principle of resistance—is greater in the big than in the small, because it is assumed there is no difference in the agent and the recipient except that. And the Imam (al-Razi) answered: that nature is a force flowing in the body, divisible by its division, so it is greater in the big, and more capable of increasing resistance. And the philosophers claim that it is a constant thing, not something that intensifies and weakens or decreases and increases in a single body, such that the nature of all water and some of it is one. And the truth of that is not apparent except by knowing the reality of what is meant by 'nature' here. And they (the philosophers) have not added beyond that nature may be said of that from which motion and rest proceed primarily and essentially without awareness and will, and may be said of that from which something proceeds that does not lag and does not depend on an external cause, like the descent of a stone downward, and may be specific to that from which motions proceed on a single course without awareness, and every bodily force may be called nature. None of that yields knowledge of its reality. As for its application to temperament, or to the dominant quality among opposing qualities, or to innate heat, or to the vegetative soul, or the like, as the physicians mention, it is specific to composites.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage presents two related arguments and the Imam's response. The first argument explains why a stone thrown upward slows near the apex and then falls faster: forced inclination weakens due to air resistance, while natural inclination strengthens until it overcomes. The second argument proves the existence of natural inclination by contrasting two stones of different sizes thrown with equal force; the larger stone moves slower because its natural inclination (which acts as resistance to forced motion) is greater due to its larger mass. The Imam (Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) responds that nature is a divisible force pervading the body, so indeed the larger stone has more nature and thus more resistance. The philosophers, however, claim nature is indivisible and uniform, e.g., all water has the same nature. The author (Taftazani) dismisses this, noting that the term 'nature' has multiple definitions (e.g., source of motion without awareness, uniform motion, etc.) but none provide a true essence. He distinguishes the philosophers' usage from that of physicians, who apply nature to composite things like temperament. The conclusion is that the meaning of nature must be clarified to resolve the debate.

Key Terms
طبيعة (ṭabīʿa)
مبدأ المدافعة (mabda' al-mudāfaʿa)
قسر (qasr)
طبع (ṭabʿ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the stone's return is purely due to external forces like gravity without an internal principle.
Correction: The passage argues that the stone has an internal natural inclination that resists forced motion and eventually prevails.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the stone's forced motion weaken?
A) Because natural inclination cancels it out
B) Because of collisions with air
C) Because the stone's nature changes
D) Because the stone loses energy

Key Takeaway
The stone's motion pattern proves an internal natural inclination that opposes forced motion and ultimately prevails.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel forced to act against your true nature—whether by peer pressure, societal expectations, or your own desires—where do you find that inner principle of resistance? Have you experienced it growing stronger as the forced pressure weakens? What would it take for you to let your true nature prevail, like the stone finally falling?
?
The stone's forced motion weakens due to collisions. What are the 'collisions' in your life—hardships, reminders, consequences—that gradually erode your resistance to returning to what is natural and right? Are you allowing them to do their work, or are you resisting the resistance?
?
The stone's natural inclination is always present, even when forced upward. In your daily life, what is the one natural inclination of your soul (toward truth, justice, worship) that you have been suppressing? What would it look like to let it prevail today?

The Two-Stone Argument and al-Razi's Response
The second argument for natural inclination and the Imam's response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the differing speeds of stones of different sizes prove that nature is a divisible force, and how Imam al-Razi counters the philosophers' objection.

Core Idea
Size affects motion because nature divides with the body.

The philosophers present a second proof: two stones thrown with equal force but different sizes move at different speeds. They argue that the difference is due to natural inclination being greater in the larger stone. Imam al-Razi confirms this but adds that nature is a divisible force, contrary to the philosophers who claim nature is indivisible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about two stones
Read the Arabic for the second argument and the Imam's response. Note how the argument uses the assumption of equal force and differing sizes.
2
Understand the reasoning
Why does the larger stone move slower? Because its natural inclination (which resists forced motion) is greater. The passage says that except for the difference in size, all conditions are equal.
3
Examine the Imam's answer
Al-Razi says nature is a force that flows through the body and divides as the body divides. Therefore, a larger body has more nature and more resistance, which explains the slower speed.
4
Contrast with the philosophers' view
The philosophers think nature is indivisible and constant, so size should not affect motion. This sets up the debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها ما ذكروا في بيان سبب أن الحجر الذي يتحرك صاعداً. بالقسر، ثم ) يرجع هابطاً بالطبع، أن حركته القسرية تشتد ابتداء وتضعف عند القرب من النهاية، والطبيعية بالعكس، لأن ميله القسري يزداد ضعفاً بمصاكات تتصل عليه من مقاومة الهواء المخروق، فيزداد الميل الطبيعي أعني مبدأ المدافعة قوة إلى أن يتعادلا ثم يأخذ القسري في الانتقاص، والطبيعي في الغلبة فيأخذ حركته في الاشتداد ومنها استدلالهم على وجود الميل الطبيعي بأن الحجرين المرميين بقوة واحدة إذا اختلفا في الصغر والكبر اختلفت حركتاهما في السرعة والبطء وليس ذاك إلا لكون المقاوم الذي هو الميل الطبيعي أعني مبدأ المدافعة في الكبير أكثر منه في الصغير، لأن التقدير عدم التفاوت في الفاعل والقابل إلا بذلك . وأجاب الإمام : بأن الطبيعة قوة سارية في الجسم منقسمة بانقسامه فيكون في الكبير أكثر، وبزيادة المقاومة أجدر والفلاسفة يزعمون أنه أمر ثابت ليس مما يشتد ويضعف أو يقل ويكثر في الجسم الواحد حتى إن (۳) طبيعة كل الماء وبعضه واحد، ولا يتبين الحق من ذلك إلا بمعرفة حقيقة ما هو المراد بالطبيعة ها هنا، وهم لم يزيدوا على أن الطبيعة قد يقال لما يصدر عنه الحركة والسكون أولاً، وبالذات دون شعور وإرادة، وقد يقال لما تصدر عنه أمر لا تتخلف عنه ، ولا يفتقر الصدور إلى علة خارجة عنه كنزول الحجر إلى السفل، وقد يخص بما يصدر عنه الحركات على نهج واحد دون شعور، وقد تسمى كل قوة جسمانية طبيعة ، وشيء من ذلك لا يفيد معرفة حقيقية، وأما إطلاقها على المزاج، أو على الكيفية الغالبة ( ) من الكيفيات المتضادة، أو على الحرارة الغريزية، أو على النفس النباتية، أو نحو ذلك (ه) على ما يذكره الأطباء فمختص بالمركبات .
And among them is what they mentioned in explaining the reason that the stone which moves upward by force then returns downward by nature: that its forced motion intensifies at the beginning and weakens near the end, and the natural motion is opposite, because its forced inclination increasingly weakens due to collisions connected to it from the resistance of the air that is cut, so the natural inclination—that is, the principle of resistance—increases in strength until they become equal, then the forced begins to decrease and the natural to prevail, so its motion begins to intensify. And among them is their proof for the existence of natural inclination: that two stones thrown with equal force, if they differ in smallness and bigness, their motions differ in speed and slowness, and that is not except because the resistant—which is natural inclination, i.e., the principle of resistance—is greater in the big than in the small, because it is assumed there is no difference in the agent and the recipient except that. And the Imam (al-Razi) answered: that nature is a force flowing in the body, divisible by its division, so it is greater in the big, and more capable of increasing resistance. And the philosophers claim that it is a constant thing, not something that intensifies and weakens or decreases and increases in a single body, such that the nature of all water and some of it is one. And the truth of that is not apparent except by knowing the reality of what is meant by 'nature' here. And they (the philosophers) have not added beyond that nature may be said of that from which motion and rest proceed primarily and essentially without awareness and will, and may be said of that from which something proceeds that does not lag and does not depend on an external cause, like the descent of a stone downward, and may be specific to that from which motions proceed on a single course without awareness, and every bodily force may be called nature. None of that yields knowledge of its reality. As for its application to temperament, or to the dominant quality among opposing qualities, or to innate heat, or to the vegetative soul, or the like, as the physicians mention, it is specific to composites.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage presents two related arguments and the Imam's response. The first argument explains why a stone thrown upward slows near the apex and then falls faster: forced inclination weakens due to air resistance, while natural inclination strengthens until it overcomes. The second argument proves the existence of natural inclination by contrasting two stones of different sizes thrown with equal force; the larger stone moves slower because its natural inclination (which acts as resistance to forced motion) is greater due to its larger mass. The Imam (Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) responds that nature is a divisible force pervading the body, so indeed the larger stone has more nature and thus more resistance. The philosophers, however, claim nature is indivisible and uniform, e.g., all water has the same nature. The author (Taftazani) dismisses this, noting that the term 'nature' has multiple definitions (e.g., source of motion without awareness, uniform motion, etc.) but none provide a true essence. He distinguishes the philosophers' usage from that of physicians, who apply nature to composite things like temperament. The conclusion is that the meaning of nature must be clarified to resolve the debate.

Key Terms
مبدأ المدافعة (mabda' al-mudāfaʿa)
قوة سارية (quwwa sāriya)
مقاومة (muqāwama)
انقسام (inqisām)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the larger stone should move faster because it's heavier, but here it's about resistance to forced motion.
Correction: The larger stone has more natural inclination, which resists forced motion, making it slower.

Checkpoint
What is the key point of the Imam's response regarding nature?
A) Nature is indivisible
B) Nature is a force that divides with the body
C) Nature is constant in all bodies
D) Nature is not affected by size

Key Takeaway
The two-stone argument shows that natural inclination varies with size, supporting the view that nature is a divisible force.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The larger stone resists motion more because it has more natural inclination. What is the 'natural inclination' in your heart that resists turning to Allah? Is it greater when your ego is large? When have you felt that your own 'bigness' (pride, knowledge, status) made you slower to submit to truth?
?
Al-Razi says nature divides with the body. Do you treat your faith as a fixed, indivisible essence, or do you allow it to be distributed among all your actions? How can you actively 'divide' your devotion to cover every part of your life starting today?
?
The philosophers argued that greater resistance proves natural inclination. How often do you resist God's call because of your own 'natural inclination'? What is the size of your resistance right now, and are you willing to ask Allah to shrink it?

Critique of the Philosophers' Concept of Nature
The author critiques the philosophers' definitions of nature and distinguishes their usage from physicians'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author dismisses the philosophers' definitions of nature as insufficient and why clarifying the term is essential for the debate.

Core Idea
Without a clear definition, the debate on nature is meaningless.

Here the passage moves from proofs to a conceptual critique. The philosophers claim nature is indivisible and constant, but the author (Taftazani) points out that the term 'nature' is ambiguous with many definitions, none of which reveal its true essence. He also distinguishes the philosophers' usage from the physicians'.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' claim
They claim nature is a constant thing that does not intensify or weaken in a single body, using water as an example.
2
Examine the author's critique
The author says that to judge the truth, we need to know the reality of 'nature'. The philosophers offer multiple definitions, but none give a true essence.
3
List the definitions given
The passage enumerates several meanings: source of motion without awareness, something that produces invariable effects, etc. Note each one.
4
Understand the final distinction
The author says that when physicians use 'nature', it applies specifically to composites (e.g., temperament, heat), not to simple bodies.

The Dual Components of Motion: Natural and Forced Impulse
Analysis of a stone's motion into natural and forced components.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the motion of a thrown stone consists of a natural downward impulse inherent to its nature and an additional forced impulse from the thrower.

Core Idea
Every motion combines a natural inclination and an external forced impulse.

Before entering deeper debates on causality, the author begins with a concrete example. A stone thrown downwards demonstrates two distinct sources of motion. Understanding this distinction is the first step in grasping the kalam theory of inclination.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two impulses
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the stone has a natural downward impulse and another impulse generated by the thrower.
2
Note the effect of the forced impulse on speed
The passage states that the stone moves faster when thrown because of the additional forced impulse. Observe how the author attributes this to the thrower's force.
3
Recognize that speed variation depends on the impeller's force
The author emphasizes that speed varies with the force of the impeller. This introduces the idea that external factors can augment natural motion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجر المدفوع إلى أسفل، فإن فيه مدافعة هابطة يقتضيها الحجر إذا خلى وطبعه وأخرى أحدثها فيه القاسر على حسب قوته وقصده، ولهذا يكون حركته حينئذ أسرع مما إذا سقط بنفسه فهبط، وتتفاوت تلك السرعة بتفاوت قوة القاسر. ومنها ما ذكروا في بيان سبب أن الحجر الذي يتحرك صاعداً بالقسر، ثم يرجع هابطاً بالطبع، أن حركته القسرية تشتد ابتداء وتضعف عند القرب من النهاية، والطبيعية بالعكس، لأن ميله القسري يزداد ضعفاً بمصاكات تتصل عليه من مقاومة الهواء المخروق، فيزداد الميل الطبيعي أعني مبدأ المدافعة قوة إلى أن يتعادلا ثم يأخذ القسري في الانتقاص، والطبيعي في الغلبة فيأخذ حركته في الاشتداد. ومنها استدلالهم على وجود الميل الطبيعي بأن الحجرين المرميين بقوة واحدة إذا اختلفا في الصغر والكبر اختلفت حركتاهما في السرعة والبطء وليس ذاك إلا لكون المقاوم الذي هو الميل الطبيعي أعني مبدأ المدافعة في الكبير أكثر منه في الصغير، لأن التقدير عدم التفاوت في الفاعل والقابل إلا بذلك. وأجاب الإمام : بأن الطبيعة قوة سارية في الجسم منقسمة بانقسامه فيكون في الكبير أكثر، وبزيادة المقاومة أجدر والفلاسفة يزعمون أنه أمر ثابت ليس مما يشتد ويضعف أو يقل ويكثر في الجسم الواحد حتى إن طبيعة كل الماء وبعضه واحد، ولا يتبين الحق من ذلك إلا بمعرفة حقيقة ما هو المراد بالطبيعة ها هنا، وهم لم يزيدوا على أن الطبيعة قد يقال لما يصدر عنه الحركة والسكون أولاً، وبالذات دون شعور وإرادة، وقد يقال لما تصدر عنه أمر لا تتخلف عنه، ولا يفتقر الصدور إلى علة خارجة عنه كنزول الحجر إلى السفل، وقد يخص بما يصدر عنه الحركات على نهج واحد دون شعور، وقد تسمى كل قوة جسمانية طبيعة، وشيء من ذلك لا يفيد معرفة حقيقية، وأما إطلاقها على المزاج، أو على الكيفية الغالبة من الكيفيات المتضادة، أو على الحرارة الغريزية، أو على النفس النباتية، أو نحو ذلك على ما يذكره الأطباء فمختص بالمركبات.
The stone thrown downwards: it has a downward impulse (mudāfaʿa) required by the stone if left to its nature, and another generated in it by the impeller according to its force and intention. Hence its motion at that moment is faster than if it fell by itself, and that speed varies with the variation of the impeller's force. Among [their arguments] is what they mentioned in explaining why the stone that moves upward by force then returns downwards by nature: its forced motion intensifies at the beginning and weakens near the end, while natural motion is the opposite, because its forced inclination (al-mayl al-qasrī) increasingly weakens through resistances that confront it from the resistance of the air cut through, so the natural inclination (al-mayl al-ṭabīʿī)—i.e., the principle of impulse (mabdaʾ al-mudāfaʿa)—increases in strength until they become equal, then the forced [inclination] begins to diminish and the natural [inclination] prevails, so its motion intensifies. And among them is their argument for the existence of natural inclination: that two stones thrown with the same force, if they differ in smallness and largeness, their motions differ in speed and slowness—this is only because the resistance, which is natural inclination—i.e., the principle of impulse—is more in the larger than in the smaller, since it is assumed there is no difference in agent and recipient except for that. The Imam replied that nature (al-ṭabīʿa) is a power diffused in the body, divisible by its division, so it is more in the larger, and is more deserving of increased resistance. The philosophers claim that it is a fixed thing, not something that intensifies and weakens or decreases and increases in a single body, such that the nature of all water and part of it is one. The truth in that cannot be known except by knowing the true reality of what is meant by nature here. They did not add more than that nature may be said of that from which motion and rest emanate primarily and essentially without consciousness and will; and may be said of that from which a thing emanates that does not depend on an external cause, like the descent of a stone downwards; and may be specified for that from which motions proceed on a single course without consciousness; and every corporeal power may be called nature. None of that conveys genuine knowledge. As for its application to temperament (al-mizāj), or to the predominant quality among opposite qualities, or to innate heat, or to the vegetative soul, or the like as the physicians mention—that is specific to composites.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage presents a sustained discussion on the concept of nature (al-ṭabīʿa) and natural inclination (al-mayl al-ṭabīʿī) in the context of motion, likely within the broader kalam debate on causality. The author begins by describing the motion of a stone thrown downwards, noting that it has two components: a natural downward impulse (mudāfaʿa hābita) inherent to the stone when left alone, and an additional impulse imparted by the thrower (al-qāsir). This explains why the stone's motion is faster when thrown than when falling freely, and why its speed varies with the force of the thrower. Next, the author presents an argument regarding a stone thrown upward by force (qasran) that then returns downward by nature (bi-l-ṭabʿi): forced motion is initially strong but weakens near the end, while natural motion is the opposite. This is because the forced inclination (al-mayl al-qasrī) weakens due to resistance from the air, while the natural inclination (al-mayl al-ṭabīʿī)—defined as the principle of impulse (mabdaʾ al-mudāfaʿa)—grows stronger until it overcomes the forced inclination, causing the stone to accelerate downward. A further argument for the existence of natural inclination is given: two stones of different sizes thrown with equal force move at different speeds because the larger stone has more natural inclination (i.e., more resistance to forced motion), assuming all other factors are equal. The Imam (likely Fakhr al-Dīn al-Rāzī or al-Ghazālī) responds by asserting that nature is a power (quwwa) diffused through the body and divisible with it, so it is greater in larger bodies and thus better able to resist. The philosophers, by contrast, consider nature a fixed, indivisible entity that does not intensify or weaken in a single body—e.g., the nature of all water is one. The author then notes that the truth of the matter cannot be determined without knowing the precise meaning of 'nature' here, and he critiques the philosophers for offering only nominal definitions: nature as that which causes motion and rest without consciousness; nature as that which produces an effect without an external cause (like the stone's fall); nature as that which produces regular motions without consciousness; or nature as any corporeal power. None of these conveys the true essence (ḥaqīqa) of nature. Finally, the author distinguishes these usages from the medical sense of nature as temperament, predominant quality, innate heat, vegetative soul, etc., which apply only to composites (murakkabāt).

D) because of the stone's weight

Key Takeaway
The motion of a thrown stone combines a natural downward impulse and a forced impulse from the thrower.

Do you sometimes act as if your natural efforts are enough, forgetting that true progress requires both your innate disposition and the added grace from God? Reflect on a time when you experienced a 'faster motion' spiritually—what was the source of that extra impulse, and how can you sustain it?

The Return of the Stone: Forced Motion Weakening, Natural Motion Strengthening
Explanation of forced motion weakening and natural motion strengthening in a projectile.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the process by which a stone thrown upward has its forced motion weaken and natural motion strengthen, leading to its descent.

Core Idea
Forced motion fades, natural motion grows, and the stone inevitably returns.

The author now explains why a stone thrown upward eventually turns around. The key lies in the interplay between forced inclination and natural inclination, with air resistance playing a role.

Guided Path
1
Observe the pattern of motion
Notice that forced motion is initially strong but weakens near the top, while natural motion is the opposite – starting weak and then strengthening.
2
Understand the role of air resistance
The forced inclination weakens due to continuous resistance from the air. This allows the natural inclination to grow until it overcomes the forced inclination.
3
Connect to the principle of impulse (mabdaʾ al-mudāfaʿa)
The natural inclination is identified as the principle of impulse. As it becomes stronger, it eventually dominates, causing the stone to accelerate downward.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجر المدفوع إلى أسفل، فإن فيه مدافعة هابطة يقتضيها الحجر إذا خلى وطبعه وأخرى أحدثها فيه القاسر على حسب قوته وقصده، ولهذا يكون حركته حينئذ أسرع مما إذا سقط بنفسه فهبط، وتتفاوت تلك السرعة بتفاوت قوة القاسر. ومنها ما ذكروا في بيان سبب أن الحجر الذي يتحرك صاعداً بالقسر، ثم يرجع هابطاً بالطبع، أن حركته القسرية تشتد ابتداء وتضعف عند القرب من النهاية، والطبيعية بالعكس، لأن ميله القسري يزداد ضعفاً بمصاكات تتصل عليه من مقاومة الهواء المخروق، فيزداد الميل الطبيعي أعني مبدأ المدافعة قوة إلى أن يتعادلا ثم يأخذ القسري في الانتقاص، والطبيعي في الغلبة فيأخذ حركته في الاشتداد. ومنها استدلالهم على وجود الميل الطبيعي بأن الحجرين المرميين بقوة واحدة إذا اختلفا في الصغر والكبر اختلفت حركتاهما في السرعة والبطء وليس ذاك إلا لكون المقاوم الذي هو الميل الطبيعي أعني مبدأ المدافعة في الكبير أكثر منه في الصغير، لأن التقدير عدم التفاوت في الفاعل والقابل إلا بذلك. وأجاب الإمام : بأن الطبيعة قوة سارية في الجسم منقسمة بانقسامه فيكون في الكبير أكثر، وبزيادة المقاومة أجدر والفلاسفة يزعمون أنه أمر ثابت ليس مما يشتد ويضعف أو يقل ويكثر في الجسم الواحد حتى إن طبيعة كل الماء وبعضه واحد، ولا يتبين الحق من ذلك إلا بمعرفة حقيقة ما هو المراد بالطبيعة ها هنا، وهم لم يزيدوا على أن الطبيعة قد يقال لما يصدر عنه الحركة والسكون أولاً، وبالذات دون شعور وإرادة، وقد يقال لما تصدر عنه أمر لا تتخلف عنه، ولا يفتقر الصدور إلى علة خارجة عنه كنزول الحجر إلى السفل، وقد يخص بما يصدر عنه الحركات على نهج واحد دون شعور، وقد تسمى كل قوة جسمانية طبيعة، وشيء من ذلك لا يفيد معرفة حقيقية، وأما إطلاقها على المزاج، أو على الكيفية الغالبة من الكيفيات المتضادة، أو على الحرارة الغريزية، أو على النفس النباتية، أو نحو ذلك على ما يذكره الأطباء فمختص بالمركبات.
The stone thrown downwards: it has a downward impulse (mudāfaʿa) required by the stone if left to its nature, and another generated in it by the impeller according to its force and intention. Hence its motion at that moment is faster than if it fell by itself, and that speed varies with the variation of the impeller's force. Among [their arguments] is what they mentioned in explaining why the stone that moves upward by force then returns downwards by nature: its forced motion intensifies at the beginning and weakens near the end, while natural motion is the opposite, because its forced inclination (al-mayl al-qasrī) increasingly weakens through resistances that confront it from the resistance of the air cut through, so the natural inclination (al-mayl al-ṭabīʿī)—i.e., the principle of impulse (mabdaʾ al-mudāfaʿa)—increases in strength until they become equal, then the forced [inclination] begins to diminish and the natural [inclination] prevails, so its motion intensifies. And among them is their argument for the existence of natural inclination: that two stones thrown with the same force, if they differ in smallness and largeness, their motions differ in speed and slowness—this is only because the resistance, which is natural inclination—i.e., the principle of impulse—is more in the larger than in the smaller, since it is assumed there is no difference in agent and recipient except for that. The Imam replied that nature (al-ṭabīʿa) is a power diffused in the body, divisible by its division, so it is more in the larger, and is more deserving of increased resistance. The philosophers claim that it is a fixed thing, not something that intensifies and weakens or decreases and increases in a single body, such that the nature of all water and part of it is one. The truth in that cannot be known except by knowing the true reality of what is meant by nature here. They did not add more than that nature may be said of that from which motion and rest emanate primarily and essentially without consciousness and will; and may be said of that from which a thing emanates that does not depend on an external cause, like the descent of a stone downwards; and may be specified for that from which motions proceed on a single course without consciousness; and every corporeal power may be called nature. None of that conveys genuine knowledge. As for its application to temperament (al-mizāj), or to the predominant quality among opposite qualities, or to innate heat, or to the vegetative soul, or the like as the physicians mention—that is specific to composites.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage presents a sustained discussion on the concept of nature (al-ṭabīʿa) and natural inclination (al-mayl al-ṭabīʿī) in the context of motion, likely within the broader kalam debate on causality. The author begins by describing the motion of a stone thrown downwards, noting that it has two components: a natural downward impulse (mudāfaʿa hābita) inherent to the stone when left alone, and an additional impulse imparted by the thrower (al-qāsir). This explains why the stone's motion is faster when thrown than when falling freely, and why its speed varies with the force of the thrower. Next, the author presents an argument regarding a stone thrown upward by force (qasran) that then returns downward by nature (bi-l-ṭabʿi): forced motion is initially strong but weakens near the end, while natural motion is the opposite. This is because the forced inclination (al-mayl al-qasrī) weakens due to resistance from the air, while the natural inclination (al-mayl al-ṭabīʿī)—defined as the principle of impulse (mabdaʾ al-mudāfaʿa)—grows stronger until it overcomes the forced inclination, causing the stone to accelerate downward. A further argument for the existence of natural inclination is given: two stones of different sizes thrown with equal force move at different speeds because the larger stone has more natural inclination (i.e., more resistance to forced motion), assuming all other factors are equal. The Imam (likely Fakhr al-Dīn al-Rāzī or al-Ghazālī) responds by asserting that nature is a power (quwwa) diffused through the body and divisible with it, so it is greater in larger bodies and thus better able to resist. The philosophers, by contrast, consider nature a fixed, indivisible entity that does not intensify or weaken in a single body—e.g., the nature of all water is one. The author then notes that the truth of the matter cannot be determined without knowing the precise meaning of 'nature' here, and he critiques the philosophers for offering only nominal definitions: nature as that which causes motion and rest without consciousness; nature as that which produces an effect without an external cause (like the stone's fall); nature as that which produces regular motions without consciousness; or nature as any corporeal power. None of these conveys the true essence (ḥaqīqa) of nature. Finally, the author distinguishes these usages from the medical sense of nature as temperament, predominant quality, innate heat, vegetative soul, etc., which apply only to composites (murakkabāt).

المدافعة
مقاومة الهواء

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have seen how forced motion inevitably fades and natural inclination takes over. What 'forced motions' in your own life—habits, pressures, bad influences—are you still allowing to carry you upward? Are you aware that they are weakening day by day, and that your true nature (fitrah) is waiting to pull you back toward your Lord? What air resistances—distractions, excuses, sin—are you cutting through that only accelerate your return to meaninglessness? Will you stop fighting your natural pull toward truth and let it take its course, or will you keep wasting energy on what cannot last?
?
The stone’s forced motion is strongest at the start but fades quickly. Which spiritual ‘launches’ have you made with great enthusiasm only to lose steam? Did you expect the upward journey to be sustained without effort against resistance? Are you now at the point of equilibrium, feeling the pull of your natural inclination toward Allah but still clinging to the fading force of your old habits? Will you let go and allow your innate longing for Him to accelerate you back to Him, or will you resist until the end?
?
The philosopher describes natural inclination as growing stronger until it dominates. Your fitrah is calling you home, but you keep silencing it with forced distractions. What have you been actively choosing that denies your natural gravitational pull toward worship and obedience? When will you stop pretending that the stone can defy its own nature, and embrace the return that is written into your very being?

The Nature of Nature: Divisibility and the Philosophers' Definitions
Argument that nature is a divisible power and critique of nominal definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate on whether nature is a fixed or divisible essence, and why the philosophers' definitions fail to capture its reality.

Core Idea
Nature is a divisible power, not a fixed essence, contrary to the philosophers' claim.

The discussion now moves from specific examples to a direct critique. The Imam (likely Razi) argues that nature is a power diffused through the body, capable of increase and decrease. The philosophers, however, insist it is one and indivisible. This leads to an examination of their various definitions, all of which are found wanting.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument from differing stone speeds
The philosophers argue that natural inclination exists because stones of different sizes thrown with equal force move at different speeds, due to more natural inclination in larger stones.
2
Understand the Imam's reply
The Imam responds that nature is a power diffused in the body, divisible by its division, so it is greater in larger bodies and thus provides more resistance.
3
Contrast with the philosophers' view
The philosophers claim nature is fixed and does not intensify or weaken, e.g., the nature of all water is one.
4
Review the four definitions and see why they fail
The author lists four nominal definitions of nature from the philosophers and concludes that none provides true knowledge of its essence.
This includes definitions like 'that from which motion emanates without consciousness' and 'every corporeal power'.

Ibn Sina on Whiteness: Denial or Ignorance?
Clarifying Ibn Sina's actual position on the cause of whiteness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what Ibn Sina actually claimed about the cause of whiteness in transparent substances and why his statement does not constitute sophistry.

Core Idea
Ibn Sina didn't deny whiteness; he simply didn't know its cause.

In this passage, the author defends Ibn Sina against the accusation of denying the existence of whiteness. By examining the causal mechanisms of whiteness, we see that Ibn Sina's position was one of epistemic humility, not denial. The lesson will unpack his argument and the misinterpretation by al-Mawaqif.

Guided Path
1
Identify the causal mechanisms discussed
Notice that the passage begins by rejecting two proposed causes for whiteness: separation with air penetration, and approximation with light reflection.
2
Understand the argument for transformation
Read the example of gypsum: whiteness occurs by cooking with fire, not by grinding. This shows transformation (istihala) as the real cause.
3
Read Ibn Sina's actual statement
The passage quotes Ibn Sina from al-Shifa: 'I do not know whether whiteness occurs by another cause or not.' This is an expression of ignorance, not denial.
4
Recognize the misinterpretation
Al-Mawaqif misread Ibn Sina's statement as a denial, leading to an accusation of sophistry. The passage corrects this by showing Ibn Sina's intent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سبب فيه وما ذاك بحدوث تفرق في شفاف ونفوذ هواء فيه فإنه كان متفرقاً منحلاً في الخل، ولا لتقارب أجزاء متفرقة وانعكاس ضوء البعض إلى البعض لأن حدة ماء القلى بالتفريق أولى، بل ذلك على سبيل الاستحالة، كما في الجص فإنه يبيض بالطبخ بالنار ولا يبيض بالسحق، والتصوفل (۱) مع أن تفريق الأجزاء ومداخلة الهواء فيه أظهر فظهر أن ابن سينا لم ينكر حصول البياض في الثلج ، وزبد الماء، ومسحوق البلور، والزجاج، ونحو ذلك مما لا سوى مخالطة الهواء بالمشف بل ادعى حصوله بأسباب أخر بعد ما كان لا يعلم حصوله إلا بهذا السبب على ما قال في موضع من الشفاء(۲). (لا أعلم هل يحصل البياض بسبب آخر أم لا؟ وكان صاحب المواقف فهم ـ وحاشاه عن سوء الفهم - من بعض عبارات الشفاء حيث يقول) (۳) وفي بيان سبب البياض في الصور المذكورة : أن اختلاط الهواء بالمشف على الوجه المخصوص سبب لظهور لون أبيض ولرؤية لون هو البياض، إنه ينكر وجود البياض فيها بالحقيقة فنسبة إلى السفسطة (٤)
It is caused by that? And that is not due to the occurrence of separation in a transparent object and the penetration of air into it, because it (the transparent object) was separate and dissolved in vinegar. Nor due to the approximation of separate parts and the reflection of light from some to others, because the sharpness of potash water is more likely to cause separation. Rather, it is by way of transformation (istihala), as in gypsum: it becomes white by cooking with fire and does not become white by grinding and pulverization, even though the separation of parts and the entry of air into it are more apparent. Thus it becomes clear that Ibn Sina did not deny the occurrence of whiteness in snow, foam of water, crushed crystal, glass, and the like, which have no [cause] other than mixing of air with the transparent. Rather, he claimed its occurrence by other causes after he did not know its occurrence except by this cause, as he said in a place in al-Shifa: 'I do not know whether whiteness occurs by another cause or not.' And the author of al-Mawaqif understood – far be it from him to misunderstand – from some statements of al-Shifa that he (Ibn Sina) denies the existence of whiteness in them in reality, so he attributed sophistry to him.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage clarifies Ibn Sina's actual position regarding the cause of whiteness in transparent substances. The author begins by rejecting several proposed mechanisms: separation of transparent parts with air penetration, approximation of parts with light reflection, and the sharpness of potash. Instead, he argues that whiteness occurs through transformation (istihala), using gypsum as an example—gypsum turns white by fire, not by grinding. This shows that whiteness can arise from causes other than air mixing. Ibn Sina is then defended: he did not deny whiteness in snow, foam, crushed crystal, etc., but only claimed that other causes might exist besides air mixing. The author criticizes the author of al-Mawaqif for misinterpreting Ibn Sina's statement 'I do not know...' as a denial, which led to an accusation of sophistry. The key point is that Ibn Sina's uncertainty was about alternative causes, not about the existence of whiteness itself.

Key Terms
البياض
السواد
الاستحالة
المشف
السفسطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ibn Sina denied the existence of whiteness in snow, foam, and crushed crystal.
Correction: Ibn Sina only claimed not to know whether whiteness occurs by another cause besides air mixing; he affirmed its existence.

Checkpoint
What did Ibn Sina actually claim about the cause of whiteness in snow and foam?
A) He denied that whiteness exists in them.
B) He claimed whiteness is only due to air mixing.
C) He claimed whiteness can occur by other causes besides air mixing.
D) He claimed whiteness is impossible.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina's uncertainty about the cause of whiteness does not imply denial of its existence; his view is compatible with the reality of whiteness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When did you last say 'I do not know' about a matter of faith or philosophy? How did your community react, and what does this teach you about intellectual courage?
?
Think of a person you have dismissed as a skeptic or denier simply because they expressed uncertainty. How might this passage correct your judgment, and will you seek to understand their position more generously?
?
Ibn Sina was open to the possibility of unknown causes. Is there an area of your belief where you have closed yourself to new evidence? What specific step will you take to remain honest about what you do not know?

The Dispute on the Locus of Light in Air
The author discusses the controversy over whether light inheres in pure air or its mixtures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical dispute over whether light inheres in pure air or its mixtures, including the key arguments and counterarguments.

Core Idea
The locus of light in air is contested between pure air and particles.

This passage examines a debate among philosophers about the substrate of light in the atmosphere. Some argue that the luminous quality belongs to pure air, while others maintain it arises from vaporous or smoky particles. The author presents the reasoning on both sides and offers critical evaluation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Get a sense of the two opposing views.
Notice the logical structure: consensus, then dispute, then arguments.
2
Identify the two positions
Identify what each group claims as the locus of light: pure air vs. mixed particles.
3
Analyze the first group's arguments
Examine the first group's evidence (dawn horizon, stars visibility) and the author's critique that these do not specifically pertain to pure air.
4
Examine the Imam's argument and its weakness
The Imam argues that if light were due to admixture, purer air would have less light, but the opposite is observed. Note the author's counter: there could be an optimal mixture.
5
Analyze the second group's argument and refutation
The second group argues luminous air would be visible; the refutation denies entailment because color is a condition for vision, and pure air lacks color.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: ولهم تردد في أن المضيء فيما يشاهد من الهواء هو الهواء الصرف، أو ما يخالطه من الأجزاء. لا خلاف بين المحققين من الحكماء في إضاءة الهواء، إنما الخلاف في أن محل الضوء هو نفس الهواء الصرف، أو ما يخالطه من الأجزاء البخارية أو الدخانية، أو نحو ذلك. احتج الأولون بما يشاهد من الهواء المضيء في أفق المشرق وقت الصباح، وبأنه لو لم يكن مضيئاً لوجب أن يرى بالنهار الكواكب التي في الجهة المخالفة للشمس، إذ لا مانع سوى انفعال الحس عن ضوء أقوى، وضعفهما ظاهر، لأن الكلام في الهواء الصرف والأقرب ما ذكره الإمام وهو أن إضاءة الهواء لو كان بسبب مخالطة الأجزاء لكان الهواء كلما كان أصفى كان أقل ضوءاً، وكلما كان أكدر وأغلظ فأكثر، والأمر بالعكس، وفيه أيضاً ضعف الجواز أن يكون الموجب مخالطة الأجزاء إلى حد مخصوص، إذا تجاوزته أخذ الضوء في النقصان، وحاصله أنه يجوز أن يضره الإفراط كما يضره التفريط، تمسك الآخرون بأنه لو تكيف بالضوء لوجب أن يحس به مضيئاً كالجدار، واللازم باطل لأن الهواء غير مرئي، ورد بمنع الملازمة إذ من شرائط الرؤية اللون ولا لون للهواء الصرف.
He said: And they hesitate concerning that which is luminous in what is seen of the air: is it pure air, or what mixes with it of particles? There is no disagreement among the verifiers of the philosophers about the illumination of air; the disagreement is only concerning whether the locus of light is pure air itself, or what mixes with it of vaporous or smoky particles, or the like. The first group argued by what is seen of luminous air in the eastern horizon at morning time, and by the fact that if it were not luminous, then during the day the stars in the opposite direction from the sun would necessarily be seen, since there is no obstacle except the sense's being affected by a stronger light. But both of these are weak, because the discussion concerns pure air. The closest view is what the Imam mentioned: that if the illumination of air were due to admixture of particles, then the purer the air, the less light it would have, and the more turbid and thick, the more light; but the opposite is the case. And there is also weakness in it: it is possible that the cause is admixture up to a specific limit, beyond which light begins to decrease; the upshot is that it is possible that excess harms it just as deficiency harms it. The others clung to the argument that if it were qualified by light, it would necessarily be perceived as luminous like a wall, but the consequent is false because air is invisible. This was refuted by denying the entailment, since one of the conditions of vision is color, and pure air has no color.
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute among philosophers about whether the light seen in the air resides in pure air itself or in the particles mixed with it (vapors, smoke, etc.). First, there is consensus that air becomes illuminated; the disagreement is only about the substrate. The first group cites observable luminous air at dawn, and argues that if air were not luminous, stars opposite the sun would be visible during the day. The author notes both arguments are weak because they do not specifically refer to pure air. The Imam's argument is favored: if light came from admixture, purer air would have less light, but we observe the opposite. However, even this is weak, as there could be a specific optimum mixture. The other side argues that if air were luminous it would be visible, but it is not. The reply is that vision requires color, and pure air has none, so the entailment fails.

Key Terms
المضيء
الهواء الصرف
الأجزاء البخارية
محل الضوء
شرائط الرؤية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Luminous air must be visible because light makes things visible.
Correction: Visibility requires color; pure air has no color, so even if luminous, it may not be seen.

Checkpoint
What is the refutation of the argument that if air were luminous it would be visible?
A) Air is too thin to be seen.
B) Visibility requires color, and pure air lacks color.
C) Light is not a quality.
D) Air is transparent by nature.

Key Takeaway
The dispute over the locus of light in air remains unresolved; each side's argument has been critiqued, highlighting the role of conditions like color in vision.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The scholars debated a seemingly abstract point about light and air without clear resolution. In your own life, are you willing to hold unanswered questions about your faith and the natural world, or do you demand certainty even where it isn't possible? What does this passage teach you about intellectual humility?
?
Just as the philosophers argued that air cannot be seen even if luminous, your heart may be illuminated with guidance yet others may not see it because of the 'color' of your actions. What 'color'—habit, sin, or neglect—might be preventing the light of your faith from being visible to those around you? What will you change today?
?
Both sides of this dispute had plausible arguments, yet each had weaknesses. When you are convinced of something, do you actively seek out the weaknesses in your own position, or do you cling to arguments that feel strong? Imagine you were part of this debate: which argument would you be most reluctant to abandon, and why?

The Atomistic Theory of Light and Its Refutation
Taftazani presents and immediately denies the atomistic theory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the claim that light consists of small bodies and Taftazani's immediate refutation that the apparent motion is an illusion.

Core Idea
Light does not travel; it occurs successively in the illuminated object.

Some philosophers argued that light must be a body because it appears to move from its source to what it illuminates. Taftazani rejects this, stating that what we call motion of light is merely the successive creation of light in adjacent objects. This lesson presents the theory and its general refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the claim of the philosophers
Read the Arabic passage carefully to understand the argument that light is a body because it moves.
Notice they use the example of a lamp being moved.
2
Identify Taftazani's answer
Find the line where he says 'The answer is denial' and see his explanation that all observed motion is just successive illumination.
He calls the motion an illusion.
3
Reflect on the key insight
Consider why this refutation is important: it sets the stage for the specific proofs that follow.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الرابع: لما كان حدوث الضوء في المستضيء قد يكون من مضيء ،عال أو متحرك أو متوسط بينه وبين المضيء بالذات توهم أن الضوء نفسه يتحرك انحداراً أو اتباعاً أو انعكاساً ، فهو أجسام صغار تنفصل من المضيء ويتصل بالمستضيء، ويبطله أنه لا يعقل الحركة بالطبع إلى جهات مختلفة، ولا الحركة في لحظة من فلك الشمس إلى الأرض مع خرق للأفلاك، ولا كون ما وراء الجسم المحسوس أظهر للباصرة السليمة) . زعم بعض الحكماء (۱) : أن الضوء أجسام صغار تنفصل من المضيء وتتصل بالمستضيء تمسكاً بأنه متحرك بالذات وكل متحرك بالذات جسم، أما الكبرى فظاهرة وإنما قيدنا بالذات لأن الأعراض تتحرك بتبعية المحل، وأما الصغرى فلأن الضوء ينحدر من الشمس إلى الأرض. ويتبع المضيء في الانتقال من مكان إلى مكان كما يشاهد في السراج المنقول من موضع إلى موضع ، وينعكس مما يلقاه إلى غيره، وكل ذلك حركة .
والجواب المنع بل كل ذلك حدوث للضوء في المقابل للمضيء والحركة وهم ، ويدل على بطلان هذا الرأي وجوه .
الأول : أنه لو كان جسماً متحركاً لامتنع حركته إلى جهات مختلفة ضرورة أنها ليست بالقسر والإرادة ، بل بالطبع ، والحركة بالطبع إنما تكون إلى العلو أو السفل .
الثاني : أنه لو كان جسماً لامتنع حركته في لحظة من فلك الشمس إلى الأرض، مع خرق الأفلاك التي تحته
الثالث: أنه لو كان جسماً ولا خفاء فى أنه محسوس بالبصر لكان ساتراً للجسم الذي يحيط به الضوء، فكان الأكثر ضوءاً أشد استناراً، والواقع خلافه، ولو سلم عدم لزوم الاستتار فلا خفاء في أنه مرئي حائل في الجملة، فيلزم أن يكون الأكثر ضوءاً أقل ظهوراً وأصعب رؤية لا أن يكون أعون على إدراك الباصرة السليمة، نعم ربما يستعان بالحائل على إبصار الخطوط الدقيقة عند ضعف في الباصرة، بحيث يحتاج إلى ما يجمع القوة، وقد يجاب بأن ذلك إنما هو شأن الأجسام الكثيفة لا الشفافة، وأما هذا النوع من الأجسام فإحاطته بالمرئي شرط للرؤية .
The fourth investigation: Since the occurrence of light in the illuminated object may be from a luminous source, high or moving or intermediate between it and the intrinsically luminous source, one might imagine that the light itself moves downwards or following or by reflection, so it is small bodies that separate from the luminous source and connect to the illuminated object. And it is refuted by the fact that motion by nature to different directions is not conceivable, nor motion in an instant from the sphere of the sun to the earth with penetration of the spheres, nor that what is beyond a sensible body is more apparent to the healthy sight. Some philosophers claimed that light is small bodies that separate from the luminous source and connect to the illuminated object, clinging to the fact that it is intrinsically moving, and every intrinsically moving thing is a body. As for the major premise, it is obvious. We only qualified it with 'intrinsically' because accidents move by following their subject. As for the minor premise, it is because light descends from the sun to the earth, and follows the luminous source in moving from place to place as observed in a lamp moved from one location to another, and is reflected from what it encounters to something else, and all of that is motion. The answer is denial; rather, all that is the occurrence of light in what faces the luminous source, and motion is an illusion. And proofs for the falsity of this view are several. First: If it were a moving body, its movement to different directions would be impossible, since it is not by compulsion or will, but by nature, and motion by nature is only upward or downward. Second: If it were a body, its movement in an instant from the sphere of the sun to the earth, with penetration of the spheres beneath it, would be impossible. Third: If it were a body, and it is no secret that it is perceived by sight, it would obscure the body that the light surrounds, so the more light there is, the more concealed it would be, but the reality is contrary to that. And if its failure to cause obstruction is conceded, there is no doubt that it is a visible obstacle in general, so it would follow that the more light there is, the less apparent and more difficult to see it would be, rather than being an aid to perception by the healthy sight. Yes, sometimes an obstacle is used to see fine lines when there is weakness in the sight, so that one needs something to concentrate the visual power. And it might be answered that this is only the case with dense bodies, not transparent ones; but as for this type of bodies, its surrounding the visible object is a condition for vision.
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the atomistic theory of light, which claims that light consists of small bodies (particles) that detach from the luminous source and travel to the illuminated object. The argument begins by stating the view of some philosophers: they argue that light is intrinsically moving, and every intrinsically moving thing is a body; they support the minor premise by observing light descending from the sun, following a moving lamp, and reflecting. Then the author gives the answer: outright denial. He asserts that all these phenomena are merely the successive occurrence of light in objects facing the source, and the apparent motion is an illusion. To prove the falsity of this view, he presents three proofs. The first proof: if light were a moving body, its natural motion could only be upward or downward (since motion by nature is not by compulsion or will), but light appears to move in various directions, which is impossible. The second proof: if light were a body, it could not move in an instant from the sun's sphere to the earth while penetrating the celestial spheres beneath it, an impossible speed and penetration for a body. The third proof: if light were a body, it would be visible and would obstruct the view of objects it surrounds; yet the more light there is, the more clearly we see objects, contrary to what would be expected if light were a screen. The passage then addresses a potential objection: sometimes a screen (hā'il) helps in seeing fine lines when vision is weak, but the author responds that this applies only to dense bodies, not to transparent ones like light; indeed, for this type of body (i.e., light), surrounding the visible object is a condition for vision, not an obstruction. Thus, the three proofs convincingly refute the particle theory of light.

Key Terms
ضوء
مستضيء
مضيء
جسم
حركة
متحرك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The observable movement of a shadow or the travel of light from a lamp implies that light itself is a moving body.
Correction: Taftazani clarifies that light simply occurs in successive objects facing the source; there is no actual locomotion of a physical body.

Checkpoint
What is the main argument the philosophers use to claim that light is a body?
A) Light is visible, so it must be a body.
B) Light is intrinsically moving, and every intrinsically moving thing is a body.
C) Light illuminates objects, so it must be made of particles.
D) Light comes from the sun, which is a large body.

Key Takeaway
Light is not a moving body; its apparent motion is merely the successive occurrence of light in adjacent objects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see sunlight streaming through a window, do you truly believe that Allah creates light in every point it touches, without any physical particles traveling? How does this reshape your awareness of His direct power in the most ordinary moments of your day?
?
In what areas of your life have you been deceived by the apparent continuity of things—thinking that change happens through natural processes—when in reality each moment is a direct creation from Allah? Will you commit to pausing today to recognize His hand in every single 'occurrence' of light and life around you?

First and Second Proofs Against the Particle Theory
Two proofs based on motion and instantaneous travel.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first two proofs: light cannot move naturally to different directions, and it cannot travel instantaneously through celestial spheres.

Core Idea
A body cannot move in multiple directions by nature, nor can it penetrate spheres in an instant.

After the general denial, Taftazani provides specific arguments. The first proof examines the nature of motion; the second considers the impossible speed and penetration required if light were a body. This lesson walks through both proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the first proof
Read the first proof in Arabic and translation. Understand the logic: natural motion is only upward or downward, but light appears to move in various directions.
The key term 'طبع' (nature) is important here.
2
Read the second proof
Read the second proof. Consider why instantaneous motion through spheres is impossible for a body.
Note the phrase 'خرق الأفلاك' (penetration of spheres).
3
Compare the proofs
Notice how the two proofs use different impossibilities: one based on direction, the other on speed and penetration.
Both rely on the nature of bodies in Aristotelian physics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الرابع: لما كان حدوث الضوء في المستضيء قد يكون من مضيء ،عال أو متحرك أو متوسط بينه وبين المضيء بالذات توهم أن الضوء نفسه يتحرك انحداراً أو اتباعاً أو انعكاساً ، فهو أجسام صغار تنفصل من المضيء ويتصل بالمستضيء، ويبطله أنه لا يعقل الحركة بالطبع إلى جهات مختلفة، ولا الحركة في لحظة من فلك الشمس إلى الأرض مع خرق للأفلاك، ولا كون ما وراء الجسم المحسوس أظهر للباصرة السليمة) . زعم بعض الحكماء (۱) : أن الضوء أجسام صغار تنفصل من المضيء وتتصل بالمستضيء تمسكاً بأنه متحرك بالذات وكل متحرك بالذات جسم، أما الكبرى فظاهرة وإنما قيدنا بالذات لأن الأعراض تتحرك بتبعية المحل، وأما الصغرى فلأن الضوء ينحدر من الشمس إلى الأرض. ويتبع المضيء في الانتقال من مكان إلى مكان كما يشاهد في السراج المنقول من موضع إلى موضع ، وينعكس مما يلقاه إلى غيره، وكل ذلك حركة .

الثالث: أنه لو كان جسماً ولا خفاء فى أنه محسوس بالبصر لكان ساتراً للجسم الذي يحيط به الضوء، فكان الأكثر ضوءاً أشد استناراً، والواقع خلافه، ولو سلم عدم لزوم الاستتار فلا خفاء في أنه مرئي حائل في الجملة، فيلزم أن يكون الأكثر ضوءاً أقل ظهوراً وأصعب رؤية لا أن يكون أعون على إدراك الباصرة السليمة، نعم ربما يستعان بالحائل على إبصار الخطوط الدقيقة عند ضعف في الباصرة، بحيث يحتاج إلى ما يجمع القوة، وقد يجاب بأن ذلك إنما هو شأن الأجسام الكثيفة لا الشفافة، وأما هذا النوع من الأجسام فإحاطته بالمرئي شرط للرؤية .
The fourth investigation: Since the occurrence of light in the illuminated object may be from a luminous source, high or moving or intermediate between it and the intrinsically luminous source, one might imagine that the light itself moves downwards or following or by reflection, so it is small bodies that separate from the luminous source and connect to the illuminated object. And it is refuted by the fact that motion by nature to different directions is not conceivable, nor motion in an instant from the sphere of the sun to the earth with penetration of the spheres, nor that what is beyond a sensible body is more apparent to the healthy sight. He asserts that all these phenomena are merely the successive occurrence of light in objects facing the source, and the apparent motion is an illusion. To prove the falsity of this view, he presents three proofs. The first proof: if light were a moving body, its natural motion could only be upward or downward (since motion by nature is not by compulsion or will), but light appears to move in various directions, which is impossible. The second proof: if light were a body, it could not move in an instant from the sun's sphere to the earth while penetrating the celestial spheres beneath it, an impossible speed and penetration for a body. The third proof: if light were a body, it would be visible and would obstruct the view of objects it surrounds; yet the more light there is, the more clearly we see objects, contrary to what would be expected if light were a screen. The passage then addresses a potential objection: sometimes a screen (hā'il) helps in seeing fine lines when vision is weak, but the author responds that this applies only to dense bodies, not to transparent ones like light; indeed, for this type of body (i.e., light), surrounding the visible object is a condition for vision, not an obstruction. Thus, the three proofs convincingly refute the particle theory of light.

Correction: Natural motion of a body is limited to upward or downward; light's apparent motion in various directions disproves it being a body.

Third Proof: Light as a Body Would Obstruct Vision
Third proof based on visual obstruction and the objection about transparent bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third proof that light cannot be a body because it would obstruct vision, and the refutation of the objection about transparent bodies.

Core Idea
Light aids sight, but if it were a body, more light would mean more obstruction.

The third proof takes a different approach: if light were a body, it would be visible and would block the view of objects it surrounds. In reality, more light improves vision. The passage also addresses a potential counterexample about using screens to see fine lines, clarifying that this applies only to dense bodies, not transparent ones like light.

Guided Path
1
Read the third proof
Read the proof carefully. Understand the logical consequence: if light is a body, it would act as a screen, making objects less visible as light increases.
Note the term 'ساتر' (obstructing).
2
Examine the objection and response
Read the objection that sometimes a screen helps vision (e.g., for fine lines) and Taftazani's response distinguishing dense from transparent bodies.
The response concludes that for light, surrounding the object is a condition for vision.
3
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider how this proof complements the previous two by addressing a different aspect (visibility).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الرابع: لما كان حدوث الضوء في المستضيء قد يكون من مضيء ،عال أو متحرك أو متوسط بينه وبين المضيء بالذات توهم أن الضوء نفسه يتحرك انحداراً أو اتباعاً أو انعكاساً ، فهو أجسام صغار تنفصل من المضيء ويتصل بالمستضيء، ويبطله أنه لا يعقل الحركة بالطبع إلى جهات مختلفة، ولا الحركة في لحظة من فلك الشمس إلى الأرض مع خرق للأفلاك، ولا كون ما وراء الجسم المحسوس أظهر للباصرة السليمة) . زعم بعض الحكماء (۱) : أن الضوء أجسام صغار تنفصل من المضيء وتتصل بالمستضيء تمسكاً بأنه متحرك بالذات وكل متحرك بالذات جسم، أما الكبرى فظاهرة وإنما قيدنا بالذات لأن الأعراض تتحرك بتبعية المحل، وأما الصغرى فلأن الضوء ينحدر من الشمس إلى الأرض. ويتبع المضيء في الانتقال من مكان إلى مكان كما يشاهد في السراج المنقول من موضع إلى موضع ، وينعكس مما يلقاه إلى غيره، وكل ذلك حركة .
والجواب المنع بل كل ذلك حدوث للضوء في المقابل للمضيء والحركة وهم ، ويدل على بطلان هذا الرأي وجوه .
الأول : أنه لو كان جسماً متحركاً لامتنع حركته إلى جهات مختلفة ضرورة أنها ليست بالقسر والإرادة ، بل بالطبع ، والحركة بالطبع إنما تكون إلى العلو أو السفل .
الثاني : أنه لو كان جسماً لامتنع حركته في لحظة من فلك الشمس إلى الأرض، مع خرق الأفلاك التي تحته
الثالث: أنه لو كان جسماً ولا خفاء فى أنه محسوس بالبصر لكان ساتراً للجسم الذي يحيط به الضوء، فكان الأكثر ضوءاً أشد استناراً، والواقع خلافه، ولو سلم عدم لزوم الاستتار فلا خفاء في أنه مرئي حائل في الجملة، فيلزم أن يكون الأكثر ضوءاً أقل ظهوراً وأصعب رؤية لا أن يكون أعون على إدراك الباصرة السليمة، نعم ربما يستعان بالحائل على إبصار الخطوط الدقيقة عند ضعف في الباصرة، بحيث يحتاج إلى ما يجمع القوة، وقد يجاب بأن ذلك إنما هو شأن الأجسام الكثيفة لا الشفافة، وأما هذا النوع من الأجسام فإحاطته بالمرئي شرط للرؤية .
The fourth investigation: Since the occurrence of light in the illuminated object may be from a luminous source, high or moving or intermediate between it and the intrinsically luminous source, one might imagine that the light itself moves downwards or following or by reflection, so it is small bodies that separate from the luminous source and connect to the illuminated object. And it is refuted by the fact that motion by nature to different directions is not conceivable, nor motion in an instant from the sphere of the sun to the earth with penetration of the spheres, nor that what is beyond a sensible body is more apparent to the healthy sight. Some philosophers claimed that light is small bodies that separate from the luminous source and connect to the illuminated object, clinging to the fact that it is intrinsically moving, and every intrinsically moving thing is a body. As for the major premise, it is obvious. We only qualified it with 'intrinsically' because accidents move by following their subject. As for the minor premise, it is because light descends from the sun to the earth, and follows the luminous source in moving from place to place as observed in a lamp moved from one location to another, and is reflected from what it encounters to something else, and all of that is motion. The answer is denial; rather, all that is the occurrence of light in what faces the luminous source, and motion is an illusion. And proofs for the falsity of this view are several. First: If it were a moving body, its movement to different directions would be impossible, since it is not by compulsion or will, but by nature, and motion by nature is only upward or downward. Second: If it were a body, its movement in an instant from the sphere of the sun to the earth, with penetration of the spheres beneath it, would be impossible. Third: If it were a body, and it is no secret that it is perceived by sight, it would obscure the body that the light surrounds, so the more light there is, the more concealed it would be, but the reality is contrary to that. And if its failure to cause obstruction is conceded, there is no doubt that it is a visible obstacle in general, so it would follow that the more light there is, the less apparent and more difficult to see it would be, rather than being an aid to perception by the healthy sight. Yes, sometimes an obstacle is used to see fine lines when there is weakness in the sight, so that one needs something to concentrate the visual power. And it might be answered that this is only the case with dense bodies, not transparent ones; but as for this type of bodies, its surrounding the visible object is a condition for vision.
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the atomistic theory of light, which claims that light consists of small bodies (particles) that detach from the luminous source and travel to the illuminated object. The argument begins by stating the view of some philosophers: they argue that light is intrinsically moving, and every intrinsically moving thing is a body; they support the minor premise by observing light descending from the sun, following a moving lamp, and reflecting. Then the author gives the answer: outright denial. He asserts that all these phenomena are merely the successive occurrence of light in objects facing the source, and the apparent motion is an illusion. To prove the falsity of this view, he presents three proofs. The first proof: if light were a moving body, its natural motion could only be upward or downward (since motion by nature is not by compulsion or will), but light appears to move in various directions, which is impossible. The second proof: if light were a body, it could not move in an instant from the sun's sphere to the earth while penetrating the celestial spheres beneath it, an impossible speed and penetration for a body. The third proof: if light were a body, it would be visible and would obstruct the view of objects it surrounds; yet the more light there is, the more clearly we see objects, contrary to what would be expected if light were a screen. The passage then addresses a potential objection: sometimes a screen (hā'il) helps in seeing fine lines when vision is weak, but the author responds that this applies only to dense bodies, not to transparent ones like light; indeed, for this type of body (i.e., light), surrounding the visible object is a condition for vision, not an obstruction. Thus, the three proofs convincingly refute the particle theory of light.
