Life as a Distinct Principle: Proof, Objection, and Answer
al-Sanusi presents proof, objection, and answer on the distinction of life.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proof that life is distinct from the powers of sensation and nutrition, and how al-Razi's objection is answered using the concept of actual efficacy.

Core Idea
Life is a distinct principle from the powers it activates, not identical to them.

This passage presents a classic theological argument for the distinction between life and its associated faculties. The author (al-Sanusi) provides a proof, followed by a sharp objection from Fakhr al-Din al-Razi and a precise answer. Engaging with this argument will sharpen your understanding of causality and the nature of life.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation to grasp the overall flow: proof, objection, answer.
Pay attention to the key terms: مغايرة, قوة الحس والحركة, قوة التغذية.
2
Identify the main claim and proof
The author claims life is distinct from sensation and nutrition. Find the evidence: life exists in paralyzed limbs (no sensation) and withered limbs (no nutrition).
The proof relies on the presence of life without the effects.
3
Understand al-Razi's objection
Al-Razi argues that absence of effect does not prove absence of power; there could be an obstacle (مانع) on the part of the recipient.
A common misunderstanding: the objection seems to refute the proof if unanswered.
4
Analyze the answer
The answer redefines 'power' as that which actually produces its effect (بالفعل). The power that produces life's effects remains, while the powers of sensation/nutrition are absent, so they are distinct.
This shows the importance of precise definitions in theological arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا حصل في المركب اعتدال يليق بنوع من أنواع الحيوان فاض عليه قوة الحياة فانبعثت عنها بإذن الله تعالى الحواس الظاهرة والباطنة والقوى المحركة نحو جلب المنافع ودفع المضار فتكون الحياة مشروطة باعتدال المزاج، ومبدأ القوة الحس والحركة فتغاير هما بالضرورة وكذا تغاير القوة الغاذية لوجودها في النبات بخلاف الحياة لكن هذا إنما يتم لو ثبت أن الحياة مبدأ لقوة الحس والحركة لا نفسها وأن الغاذية في النبات والحيوان حقيقة واحدة ليلزم من مغايرة تلك الحياة مغايرة هذه لها فاستدلوا على مغايرة الحياة لقوة الحس والحركة ولقوة التغذية الحيوانية بأن الحياة موجودة في العضو المفلوج للحيوان من غير حس وحركة ، وفي العضو الذابل من غير اغتذاء . واعترض الإمام بأن عدم الإحساس والحركة وعدم الاغتذاء لا يدلان على عدم قوة الحس والحركة وعدم قوة التغذية لجواز أن توجد القوة ولا يصدر عنها الأثر لمانع من جهة القابل . وأجيب بأن القوة ما يصدر عنه الأثر بالفعل بمعنى أنا نريد أن القوة التي تصدر عنها بالفعل آثار الحياة كحفظ العضو عن التعفن مثلاً ،باقية، والقوة التي يصدر عنها بالفعل الحس والحركة والتغذية غير باقية فلا تكون هي هي بهذا يشعر كلام تلخيص المحصل وليس معناه أن القوة اسم لما يصدر عنه الأثر
When a temperament suitable for a species of animals occurs in the compound, the power of life emanates upon it, and from it, by the permission of God Almighty, the external and internal senses and the motive powers for attracting benefits and repelling harms are activated. Thus life is conditioned by balanced temperament and is a principle of the power of sensation and movement. So it necessarily differs from both, and likewise it differs from the nutritive power because it exists in plants unlike life. But this is only complete if it is established that life is a principle of the power of sensation and movement, not identical to it, and that the nutritive power in plants and animals is one and the same reality, so that from the difference of that life there follows the difference of this (nutritive power) from it. So they adduced as evidence for the difference of life from the power of sensation and movement and from the animal nutritive power that life is present in the paralyzed limb of an animal without sensation and movement, and in the withered limb without nutrition. Imam (al-Razi) objected that the absence of sensation and movement and the absence of nutrition do not prove the absence of the power of sensation and movement and the nutritive power, because it is possible that the power exists but its effect does not occur due to an obstacle on the part of the recipient. It was answered that power is that from which the effect actually proceeds, meaning that we intend that the power that actually produces the effects of life, such as preserving the limb from decay, remains, while the power that actually produces sensation, movement, and nutrition is not remaining. So they are not identical. This is indicated by the statement of Talkhis al-Muhassal, and it does not mean that power is a name for that from which the effect proceeds.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage presents a proof (istidlal) that life is distinct from the powers of sensation and nutrition, followed by an objection (i'tirad) and an answer (jawab). It begins by describing the process: when balanced temperament occurs in a compound, the power of life emanates, and by God's permission, the senses and motive powers are activated. Thus life is conditioned by balanced temperament and is a principle of the power of sensation and movement, so it is necessarily distinct from them. It is also distinct from the nutritive power because nutrition exists in plants without life. However, the proof is only valid if it is established that life is a principle, not the same as the power it produces, and that the nutritive power in plants and animals is the same reality. The evidence adduced is that life is present in a paralyzed limb (عضو مفلوج) without sensation or movement, and in a withered limb (عضو ذابل) without nutrition. Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi objects that the absence of effect does not prove the absence of the power, because the power may exist but its effect is prevented by an obstacle (مانع) on the part of the recipient. The answer clarifies that by 'power' here we mean that which actually produces its effect (بالفعل). The power that actually produces life's effects (like preserving the limb from decay) remains, while the power that actually produces sensation, movement, and nutrition is absent. Therefore, they are not identical. The answer is supported by the text of Talkhis al-Muhassal, and it emphasizes that 'power' is not merely a name for that which produces an effect (i.e., it is not a tautology).

Key Terms
مغايرة
قوة الحس والحركة
قوة التغذية
العضو المفلوج
العضو الذابل
مانع
بالفعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection by al-Razi successfully disproves the proof that life is distinct from the powers of sensation and nutrition.
Correction: The answer clarifies that by 'power' we mean actual efficacy; thus the absence of sensation and nutrition in living limbs shows that the powers are distinct, because the power producing life's effects remains while the others are absent.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the objection by Imam (al-Razi) to the proof that life is distinct from the powers of sensation and nutrition?
A) The proof assumes the existence of life
B) The absence of effect does not prove the absence of the power because of a possible obstacle
C) The witness of the paralyzed limb is invalid
D) Life and the powers are identical

Key Takeaway
Life is a distinct principle from the powers of sensation and nutrition; their absence in living limbs proves they are not identical, even if a power can be impeded.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you measure your worth by your abilities—your ability to move, to feel, to produce? This lesson distinguishes life itself from the powers it activates. In a paralyzed limb, life remains even when sensation and movement are gone. If you were to lose your most treasured ability tomorrow, would you still know that your life—given directly by God—has not diminished one bit? Or are you living as if your abilities define your existence?
?
When you face a failure or loss of function—whether physical, mental, or spiritual—do you conclude that your 'life' (your very being) is somehow less? The proof in this lesson shows that life remains as a distinct principle even when its powers are absent. Where in your life are you confusing the loss of a power with the loss of life itself? What concrete step can you take today to honor the life God has given you, independent of what you can accomplish?
?
The objection of al-Razi challenges the proof by saying that a power might exist even if its effect is blocked. But the answer clarifies that the power in question is the actual source of the effect. This means that in God's sight, what matters is the reality of what is actually present, not just potential. Are you harboring any spiritual 'potential' that never produces fruit—claiming faith but without actual obedience? What effect of faith is absent from your life that you have been excusing with 'obstacles'? This lesson calls you to examine whether the power of iman is truly present in its effects, not just in name.

Correlation vs. Necessity: The Ash‘ari Response to Empirical Objections
Al-Taftazani presents the philosophers’ and Mu‘tazila’s empirical objection and the Ash‘ari response of correlation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Ash‘aris reject the claim that life is necessarily dependent on bodily structure, temperament, and spirit, based on the principle that correlation does not imply metaphysical necessity.

Core Idea
Observation shows correlation, not metaphysical necessity.

The philosophers and many Mu‘tazila argue from empirical observation that life is conditional on the body’s structure, temperament, and spirit. Al-Taftazani presents their objection and then provides the Ash‘ari response: what we observe is mere constant conjunction, not a necessary condition. This lesson unpacks that crucial distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the passage focusing on the philosophers’ empirical evidence. Note the specific bodily factors they list.
They mention structure, parts, temperament, and spirit circulation.
2
Identify the Ash‘ari response
Read the response: the observation only shows correlation (dawaran), which does not prove conditionality.
The key term is dawaran.
3
Understand the principle
Reflect on why this distinction matters. Correlation alone cannot establish metaphysical necessity because God can sustain life without these factors.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذهب الفلاسفة وكثير من المعتزلة إلى أن هذا الاشتراط بناء على ما يشاهد من زوال الحياة بانتقاض البنية وتفرق الأجزاء، وبانحراف المزاج عن الاعتدال النوعي وبعدم سريان الروح في العضو لسدة أو شدة ربط يمنع نفوذه، ورد بأن غايته الدوران وهو لا يقتضي الاشتراط بحيث يمتنع بدون تلك الأمور.
And the philosophers and many of the Mu'tazila held that this conditionality is based on what is observed of the cessation of life with the disruption of the structure, the dispersion of parts, the deviation of temperament from its proper balance, and the lack of circulation of the spirit in a limb due to an obstruction or a severe binding that prevents its penetration. And the response is that its ultimate basis is correlation (dawaran), and correlation does not imply conditionality such that [life] is impossible without those things.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage presents the objection of the philosophers (falasifa) and many Mu'tazila, who argue that life is conditional on balanced temperament, bodily structure, and animal spirit. Their evidence is empirical observation (mushāhada): life ceases when the bodily structure is disrupted, parts disperse, temperament deviates from its proper balance, or the spirit cannot circulate in a limb due to a blockage or tight binding. The author then gives the Ash'ari response: the basis of their argument is mere correlation (dawaran), i.e., a constant conjunction between life and these bodily factors. But correlation does not prove that these factors are necessary conditions (shart) such that life is impossible without them. This is a key principle in kalam: observable regularity does not imply metaphysical necessity; God may sustain life without these conditions.

Key Terms

الفلاسفة
المعتزلة
البنية
المزاج
الروح الحيواني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That empirical correlation constitutes proof of causal dependence.
Correction: The Ash'aris distinguish between correlation and metaphysical necessity; correlation alone does not mean life cannot exist without those bodily factors.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the philosophers' and Mu'tazila's claim that life is conditional on bodily factors?
A) Rational proof that life cannot exist without a body.
B) Observed correlation between life and bodily factors.
C) Scriptural texts indicating the necessity of the body.
D) Consensus of the early Muslims.

Key Takeaway
Observed correlation between life and bodily factors does not prove that those factors are necessary conditions for life.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what areas of your life have you assumed that 'this always leads to that' without considering that God could bring about a different outcome? Will you consciously surrender that assumption to His unbound power today?
?
The philosophers based their argument on what they saw—but their conclusion went beyond the evidence. When have you made a similar leap from 'this is how it usually is' to 'this must always be' in your own beliefs or actions? What would it cost you to admit that God's will is not tied to your patterns?
?
You rely on regularities every day: food nourishes, sleep restores, medicine heals. How would your trust in God deepen if you truly believed that these are not necessities but habits of His mercy? What one practical step can you take this week to depend more directly on Him rather than the means?

The Correlation Argument: Life and Bodily Structure
Taftazani presents and refutes the empirical argument for life's dependence on body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why empirical correlation does not prove necessary conditioning of life by bodily structure in kalam reasoning.

Core Idea
Correlation is not causation—especially not in kalam.

This lesson introduces the initial claim that life is conditioned by the body based on observation, and the critical response that this only shows correlation, not necessity. You'll see how kalam thinkers distinguish between what is always observed together and what is necessarily dependent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and its translation to see the claim and the reply. Note the three empirical observations cited by the philosophers.
The key term 'dawrān' (correlation) is central.
2
Identify the empirical evidence
List the three observations: dissolution of structure, deviation of temperament, and blocked spirit flow. Ask: Does this prove causation?
3
Understand the reply
The reply states that these observations only show correlation (dawrān), not that life is impossible without them. Reflect on why correlation is insufficient in kalam.
This connects to the broader principle that necessity requires rational proof, not just observation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذهب الفلاسفة وكثير من المعتزلة إلى أن هذا الاشتراط بناء على ما يشاهد من زوال الحياة بانتقاض البنية وتفرق الأجزاء، وبانحراف المزاج عن الاعتدال النوعي وبعدم سريان الروح في العضو لسدة أو شدة ربط يمنع نفوذه، ورد بأن غايته الدوران وهو لا يقتضي الاشتراط بحيث يمتنع بدون تلك الأمور.
واستدل بعض المتكلمين على امتناع كون الحياة مشروطة بالبنية بأنها لو اشترطت فإما أن تقوم بالجزئين من البنية حياة واحدة فيلزم قيام العرض بأكثر من محل واحد وقد مر بطلانه، وإما أن يقوم بكل جزء حياة، وحينئذ إما أن يكون القيام بكل جزء مشروطاً بالقيام بالآخر فيلزم الدور أو لا فيلزم الرجحان بلا مرجح لتماثل الأجزاء واتحاد حقيقة الحياة. لا يقال لا يجوز أن يقوم بالبعض فقط لأسباب مرجحة من الخارج. لأنا نقول : فيكون الحي ذلك البعض لا البنية المؤلفة .
وأجيب بأنها تقوم بالمجموع الذي هو البنية المؤلفة، وليس هذا من قيام العرض بمحلين على ما سبق، أو يقوم بكل جزء حياة، ويكون اشتراط كل بالآخر بطريق المعية دون التقدم فلا يلزم الدور المحال، أو يكون قيامها ببعض الأجزاء مشروطاً بقيام حياة بالآخر من غير عكس المرجح يوجد في الخارج، وإن لم يطلع عليه .
لا يقال : فحينئذ تكون الحياة غير مشروطة بالبنية حيث تحققت في الجزء الآخر من غير شرط لأنا نقول : عدم اشتراط قيام الحياة به بقيام حياة بالجزء الأول لا يستلزم عدم اشتراطه بوجود الجزء الأول الذي به تتحقق البنية.
And the philosophers and many of the Mu'tazila held that this condition [is] based on what is observed of the cessation of life with the dissolution of the bodily structure and the separation of parts, and with the deviation of temperament from specific equilibrium, and with the absence of the spirit's flow in the limb due to an obstruction or a strong binding that prevents its penetration. And it was replied that its utmost is correlation, and correlation does not necessitate condition such that it is impossible without those things. And some of the mutakallimun argued for the impossibility of life being conditioned by the bodily structure: that if it were conditioned, then either one life would inhere in two parts of the structure, necessitating the inherence of an accident in more than one locus, which has already been shown false; or a life would inhere in each part, and then either the inherence in each part is conditioned by the inherence in the other, necessitating circularity, or not, necessitating preponderance without a preponderant due to the similarity of parts and the unity of the reality of life. It may not be said: it is not permissible that it inhere in some parts only due to preponderant causes from outside. For we say: then that part would be the living thing, not the composite structure. And it was answered that it inheres in the aggregate which is the composite structure, and this is not of the type of inherence of an accident in two loci as mentioned; or a life inheres in each part, and the condition of each by the other is by way of concomitance (al-mu‘iyya) without priority, so that impossible circularity does not follow; or its inherence in some parts is conditioned by the inherence of a life in the other without the inverse, [and] the preponderant exists externally, even if not known. It may not be said: then life is unconditioned by the bodily structure since it is realized in the other part without condition. For we say: the absence of condition of the inherence of life in it by the inherence of life in the first part does not entail the absence of its condition by the existence of the first part through which the bodily structure is realized.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument about whether life (al-hayat) is necessarily conditioned by bodily structure (al-buniya). The author presents the view of philosophers and many Mu'tazila who assert that life is conditional on the body based on empirical observation: life ends when the body dissolves, temperament deviates, or spirit flow is blocked. The author replies that this only shows correlation (dawrān), not necessary condition. Then some mutakallimun give a proof that life cannot be conditioned by structure: if it were, then either one life would inhere in two parts (impossible because an accident cannot inhere in more than one locus), or each part would have a life. If each part has a life, then either the life in each part is conditioned by the life in the other (leading to circularity) or not (leading to preponderance without a preponderant, since parts are similar). An objection is raised: could not life inhere only in some parts due to external preponderant factors? The reply: then that part would be the living thing, not the whole structure. Then the author presents possible answers: life inheres in the composite whole (not as an accident in two loci), or each part has a life with mutual conditioning by way of concomitance (mu‘iyya) without temporal priority, avoiding circularity, or life in some parts is conditioned by life in others but not vice versa, with an external preponderant. A final objection: if life is realized in the other part without condition, then life is not conditioned by structure. The reply: the absence of condition in one part does not negate condition by the existence of the other part that constitutes the structure. The argument carefully defends the contingency of life on the body while rejecting necessary conditioning.

Key Terms
الحياة
البنية
الدوران

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Constant conjunction (correlation) necessarily implies causal conditioning.
Correction: In kalam, correlation (dawrān) is not sufficient to prove that one thing cannot exist without another. A rational proof is required to establish necessary condition.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason the author rejects the philosophers' argument?
A) The observations are not reliable.
B) Correlation does not necessitate necessary condition.
C) The body is not the subject of life.
D) Life is a substance, not an accident.

Key Takeaway
Empirical correlation alone cannot establish necessary dependence; kalam requires a rational argument for such conditioning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that because something always happens in a certain way, it must be caused by that factor? What in your life have you taken as necessary that may only be correlated? How would your trust in Allah be affected if you truly internalized that nothing in creation necessitates anything except by His will?
?
If the mutakallimun reject empirical correlation as proof of necessary dependence, then what does that mean for how you view your health and safety? Are you tempted to believe that your body's health is the direct cause of your life, rather than a sign of Allah's direct preservation?
?
Consider the philosophers' argument: life ceases when the body is disrupted. The mutakallimun agree on the correlation but deny necessity. What does that say about your own assumptions regarding the 'causal' relationship between your actions and their outcomes? Do you live as if your efforts necessitate results, or as if Allah is the only necessary cause?

The Mutakallimun's Proof: Life Cannot Be Conditioned by Structure
The mutakallimun present a reduction ad absurdum of life being conditioned by bodily structure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma that the mutakallimun use to prove life cannot be conditioned by bodily structure.

Core Idea
If life were conditioned by structure, it would either be impossible (accident in two loci) or lead to circularity.

This lesson presents the core proof of the mutakallimun. They argue that if life were conditioned by bodily structure, it would have to inhere either as one life in two parts (impossible for an accident) or as multiple lives, each in a part, leading to unacceptable consequences. This argument shows why theological reasoning must be precise about inherence and conditionality.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof
Read the Arabic and translation of the mutakallimun's argument. Notice the two options they present.
The proof is structured as a dilemma.
2
Analyze the first option
One life in two parts would mean an accident in more than one locus, which is impossible in kalam. Understand the principle that accidents inhere in a single subject.
3
Analyze the second option
Multiple lives, each in a part, leads to either circularity (if each conditions the other) or preponderance without a preponderant (if not). Consider why these are problematic.
The objection about some parts only is also addressed: then only that part is living, not the whole.

Source Text

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just reasoned that life is not conditioned by your body's structure. If your life is not a product of your physical organs, then what is keeping you alive at this very moment? Does this truth make you feel more dependent on the One who directly grants life, or do you still live as if your existence is self-sufficient? What will you change today to acknowledge that He is the sole Giver of life?
?
The mutakallimun argued that if life were tied to structure, it would lead to absurdities. Now look at your own fears: do you fear the decay of your body because you think your life will end with it? How does this argument challenge that fear? Can you genuinely say you believe your soul survives death? If not, what prevents you from living with that certainty?
?
The objection that life might inhere in some parts due to external factors was refuted: only those parts would be alive, not the whole. Consider your own self-worth: do you sometimes base it on only a few 'parts' of who you are—your appearance, skills, or achievements—rather than the whole person that God created? This lesson teaches that life is not a product of any part; so ask yourself: are you living as a whole person before God, or are you fragmented, relying on parts to define your worth? What step will you take to integrate your identity in the One who gives life to all parts?

Responses to the Mutakallimun's Proof: Possible Answers
Taftazani presents and discusses three responses that defend life's conditioning by bodily structure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three possible answers that rescue life's conditioning by structure without logical absurdity.

Core Idea
Life can be conditioned by structure if it inheres in the composite whole, or by concomitance, or by external preponderance.

After the mutakallimun's proof, the author presents several answers that defend the possibility of life being conditioned by bodily structure. These answers explore different metaphysical models: inherence in the composite, conditioning by concomitance (mu'iyya), and conditioning by external preponderant. This lesson unpacks each answer and the final objection that ties them together.

Guided Path
1
Read the three answers
Read the Arabic and translation of the answers. Notice each one begins with 'أو' (or).
These are alternative solutions, not a single answer.
2
Understand the first answer
Life inheres in the composite whole as a single accident. This avoids the problem of two loci because the whole is one subject.
The author notes this is not like inherence in two loci.
3
Understand the second answer
Each part has a life, but each life is conditioned by the other via concomitance (mu'iyya), not priority, so no circularity.
Concomitance means simultaneous existence without temporal precedence.
4
Understand the third answer and final objection
Life inheres in some parts conditioned by life in others, with an external preponderant. The objection says this makes life unconditioned in one part. The reply clarifies that conditioning is by the existence of the other part, not by its life.

And it was answered that it inheres in the aggregate which is the composite structure, and this is not of the type of inherence of an accident in two loci as mentioned; or a life inheres in each part, and the condition of each by the other is by way of concomitance (al-mu‘iyya) without priority, so that impossible circularity does not follow; or its inherence in some parts is conditioned by the inherence of a life in the other without the inverse, [and] the preponderant exists externally, even if not known. It may not be said: then life is unconditioned by the bodily structure since it is realized in the other part without condition. For we say: the absence of condition of the inherence of life in it by the inherence of life in the first part does not entail the absence of its condition by the existence of the first part through which the bodily structure is realized.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument about whether life (al-hayat) is necessarily conditioned by bodily structure (al-buniya). The author presents the view of philosophers and many Mu'tazila who assert that life is conditional on the body based on empirical observation: life ends when the body dissolves, temperament deviates, or spirit flow is blocked. The author replies that this only shows correlation (dawrān), not necessary condition. Then some mutakallimun give a proof that life cannot be conditioned by structure: if it were, then either one life would inhere in two parts (impossible because an accident cannot inhere in more than one locus), or each part would have a life. If each part has a life, then either the life in each part is conditioned by the life in the other (leading to circularity) or not (leading to preponderance without a preponderant, since parts are similar). An objection is raised: could not life inhere only in some parts due to external preponderant factors? The reply: then that part would be the living thing, not the whole structure. Then the author presents possible answers: life inheres in the composite whole (not as an accident in two loci), or each part has a life with mutual conditioning by way of concomitance (mu‘iyya) without temporal priority, avoiding circularity, or life in some parts is conditioned by life in others but not vice versa, with an external preponderant. A final objection: if life is realized in the other part without condition, then life is not conditioned by structure. The reply: the absence of condition in one part does not negate condition by the existence of the other part that constitutes the structure. The argument carefully defends the contingency of life on the body while rejecting necessary conditioning.

Key Terms
الحياة
البنية
المعية
الرجحان
المجموع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the third answer, what does life in part B depend on?
A) Life in part A
B) The existence of part A
C) The absence of a preponderant
D) The temperament of part B

Key Takeaway
The three answers show that life conditioning by structure can be defended by careful metaphysical distinctions: inherence in the whole, simultaneous conditioning, or one-way conditioning tied to the structure's parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The mutakallimun's argument sought to sever the link between life and bodily structure, which could undermine belief in bodily resurrection. But Taftazani offered three responses to preserve that link. Right now, is your certainty in the resurrection based on rational arguments like these, or on something else? If the arguments were refuted, would your faith waver? What is the true foundation of your hope that God will recreate your body after death?
?
The third response admits that we do not fully know the preponderant (al-murajjih) that conditions life. Your own life is being sustained every moment by factors beyond your perception. Do you live with the humility that you do not fully understand the mechanism of your own existence? Or do you subconsciously believe that you are self-sufficient? Will you today acknowledge the Preponderant who alone sustains you, and thank Him for a life you cannot fully explain?
?
If life can be conditioned by structure in a way that is not circular (as the second response shows), then your body and soul are intimately linked without contradiction. Does this truth change how you treat your body? Do you see your physical health as part of your spiritual wellbeing, or do you dismiss it as merely material? Since your life depends on this structure, will you honor it as a trust from the One who created it?

Objections to the Ash'ari View of Power
Author presents three objections against the Ash'ari view of power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three key objections raised against the Ash'ari account of human power, especially the problem of choice and the impossibility of the possible.

Core Idea
If choice is impossible before the act and simultaneous with it, where is the ground for possibility?

The Ash'ari position on human power was challenged by Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir and Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, who argued that power is nothing but bodily health. More fundamentally, the objector—likely an opponent like a Mu'tazili—launches three incisive arguments: choice cannot precede the act or accompany it, the act's occurrence is necessary when created, and the act is impossible when motives are equal. These objections aim to show that the Ash'ari cannot coherently ground the 'possible' (al-mumkina) in human agency.

Guided Path
1
Understand the alternative definitions
Read the opening lines where two alternative views of power are mentioned: power as a part of the possible (only in a derivative sense) and power as bodily soundness (Bishr and al-Razi). Note how this sets the stage for the objection.
The objection is not against these views but against the Ash'ari view that power is an additional quality.
2
Identify the first objection: choice before the act
The objector argues that choice before the act is invalid according to the Ash'aris (because they deny human origination), and choice with the act is impossible because non-existence cannot coexist with existence. Ask yourself: does this leave any room for choice?
3
Analyze the second objection: motion at the moment of creation
The objector states that motion occurs exactly when God creates it—so that moment is necessary, and the moment before is impossible. If so, where is the possibility for the agent to act otherwise?
This targets the Ash'ari occasionalist framework.
4
Examine the third objection: the problem of equal motives
When motives are equal, the act cannot occur; when motives differ, the preponderant act is necessary and the other impossible. Thus, the 'possible' (al-mumkina) is never realized. Trace this argument carefully.
This is a classic deterministic objection.
5
Reflect on the cumulative force
Consider how these three objections together aim to show that the Ash'ari concept of power fails to explain real human choice and possibility. This will prepare you for the answer in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما قيل : إنها بعض المقدور فإنما يصح في القدرة بمعنى المقدورية، أي كون الفعل بحيث يتمكن الفاعل منه ومن تركه وذهب بشر ابن المعتمر (۱) إلى أنها عبارة عن سلامة البنية من الآفات وإليه مال الإمام الرازي واعترض على ما ذكره القوم من أنا نميز بالضرورة بين حركتي البطش والرعشة، وما ذاك إلا بوجود صفة غير سلامة البنية توجد لبعض الأفراد دون البعض كالقدرة على لكتابة لزيد دون عمرو، وعلى بعض الأفعال دون البعض كقدرة زيد على القراءة دون الكتابة بأن الاختيار قبل الفعل باطل عندكم ومعه ممنوع لامتناع العدم حال الوجود وأيضاً حصول الحركة حال ما خلقها الله تعالى ضروری وقبله محال فأين الاختيار ؟ وأيضاً حصول الفعل (۲) عند استواء الدواعي محال وعند عدم الاستواء يجب الراجح ويمتنع المرجوح فلا تثبت الممكنة (۳) والجواب أن الضرورى هو التفرقة بمعنى التمكن من الفعل والترك بالنظر إلى نفس حركة البطش مع قطع النظر عن الأمور الخارجية بخلاف حركة المرتعش.
And what is said: that it (power) is part of the possible thing (maqdur) is only valid for power in the sense of being-possible (maqduriyya), i.e., the act being such that the agent is able to perform it and to leave it. Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir went to the view that it (power) is expression for soundness of constitution from defects, and Imam al-Razi inclined to that. And he objected to what the people mentioned—that we necessarily distinguish between the movements of force and tremor, and that is only due to the existence of a quality other than soundness of constitution existing in some individuals without others, like power over writing for Zayd without Amr, and over some acts without others like Zayd's power over reading without writing—by (saying) that choice before the act is invalid according to you, and with it is impossible because of the impossibility of non-existence at the time of existence; also, the occurrence of the movement at the time when God creates it is necessary, and before it is impossible; so where is choice? Also, the occurrence of the act when motives are equal is impossible, and when they are not equal, the preponderant is necessary and the preponderated is impossible; thus possibility (al-mumkina) is not established. And the answer: the necessary (distinction) is the differentiation in the sense of the capability of act and leaving with respect to the movement of force itself, disregarding external matters, contrary to the movement of the one trembling.
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This passage presents a significant objection to the Ash'ari account of power and its response. The author first mentions alternative views: that power is part of the possible thing (only valid in a derived sense), and Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir's view that power is simply soundness of constitution from defects (to which Imam al-Razi inclined). Then the objection is raised: the Ash'aris claim we necessarily distinguish between voluntary force and involuntary tremor based on an additional quality beyond bodily soundness. The objector attacks this by arguing that choice (ikhtiyar) before the act is invalid (according to Ash'aris because they deny human power of origination), and choice simultaneous with the act is impossible because existence cannot coexist with non-existence. Furthermore, the movement's occurrence is necessary when God creates it, and impossible before; so where is choice? Another argument: when motives are equal, occurrence of the act is impossible; when motives are unequal, the preponderant becomes necessary and the preponderated impossible; thus possibility (al-mumkina) is not established. The answer is that the necessary distinction is not based on choice as a conscious act, but on the capability (tamakkun) of performing the act and leaving it when considering the act itself (e.g., the movement of force) in abstraction from external factors such as divine creation or obstacles. This capability is directly perceived by inner sense and remains even if external conditions prevent actual effect. The one trembling lacks this capability with respect to the tremor.

Key Terms
المقدورية
التمكن
الاختيار
الدواعي
الراجح
المرجوح
الممكنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Ash'ari power is simply the ability to act without any definition of capability.
Correction: The Ash'aris define power as a quality enabling the agent to perform or leave an act, but the objection here challenges whether that really works given their occasionalist commitments.

Checkpoint
In the third objection, why is the occurrence of the act impossible when motives are equal?
A) Because the agent lacks sufficient power
B) Because the act would then be necessary
C) Because no preponderant motive exists to tip the scale
D) Because God does not create equal motives

Key Takeaway
The objections challenge whether Ash'ari occasionalism can coherently account for human choice and the reality of possibility (al-mumkina) in voluntary acts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a recent decision where you felt you had a real choice. At the moment of acting, did you experience a sense of genuine possibility—that you could have done otherwise? How does the objector's argument that choice cannot exist with the act challenge that experience? Commit to examining one such decision today and asking yourself: was that feeling of possibility real or an illusion?
?
The objector claims that when motives are unequal, the preponderant is necessary and the preponderated impossible. Can you identify a time when your stronger motive seemed to compel you, leaving no room for the weaker alternative? Does that mean you had no real choice? Reflect on whether you are comfortable with that conclusion, and decide now: will you accept that your actions are determined by the strongest motive, or will you seek to understand how choice could still be meaningful?
?
If the category of possibility (al-mumkina) is not established for human acts, what becomes of your moral responsibility? Have you ever used the excuse 'I had no choice' to avoid accountability? How does this lesson confront that excuse? Write down one area of your life where you are tempted to deny your agency, and then commit to acting as if you truly could have chosen otherwise.

The Ash'ari Response: Capability Over Choice
Author gives the final answer distinguishing capability from external conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari answer to the objections: the necessary distinction is not based on choice but on capability (tamakkun), which is directly perceived with respect to the act itself, disregarding external conditions.

Core Idea
Capability, not choice, is the direct object of inner perception.

Having presented the objections, the author now gives the definitive Ash'ari response. The key move is to reorient the discussion from 'choice' (ikhtiyar) to 'capability' (tamakkun). The necessary distinction we sense between a voluntary movement (like striking) and an involuntary tremor lies precisely in the fact that with the voluntary movement, we perceive ourselves as able to perform it or leave it, when we consider the movement in itself, ignoring external factors like divine creation or physical impediments. This capability is the true ground of human agency.

Guided Path
1
Locate the answer in the text
Find the sentence that begins with 'والجواب'. This is the author's direct answer to the objections.
2
Identify the core claim
The author says the necessary distinction is 'بمعنى التمكن من الفعل والترك بالنظر إلى نفس حركة البطش'. Understand that 'tamakkun' (capability) is the key concept.
This is not the same as choice; it is a direct perception of ability.
3
Understand the abstraction principle
The capability is considered 'مع قطع النظر عن الأمور الخارجية' – disregarding external matters. Why is this important? It means that even if God creates the act or obstacles exist, the inner sense of capability remains.
4
Contrast with the tremor
The one trembling does not have this capability with respect to the tremor. The movement is forced upon them. See how this answers the objection about necessary occurrence.
5
Reflect on the answer's strength
This answer sidesteps the objections about choice by moving the goalpost to capability. It preserves human responsibility while maintaining occasionalist metaphysics. Evaluate whether this is satisfactory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

Responses to Three Objections on Capacity
Response to three objections regarding capacity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how three objections regarding human capacity are answered in classical kalam.

Core Idea
Obligation requires possibility, not actual capacity; divine and human capacities are not similar.

In this passage, the author addresses three objections that might be raised against the doctrine of capacity (istita'a). The first is answered by an earlier discussion, the second by clarifying that obligation only requires possibility of the act, and the third by denying that divine and human capacities are alike. This lesson unpacks each response.

Guided Path
1
Understand the first objection's answer
The text says the first objection has been answered by what preceded. Consider why the author refers back rather than repeating the argument.
This shows the interconnected nature of kalam arguments.
2
Analyze the second response
Focus on the phrase 'we do not stipulate in the obligated person that the object of capacity be actual, but rather possible.' Reflect on the example of the disbeliever's faith.
Note that possibility is key, not whether the person can act right now.
3
Examine the third response
The author denies the similarity of capacities. Think about why someone might think they are similar and what the difference is.
This touches on the distinction between divine and human power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأجيب عن الأول بما سبق . وعن الثاني : بأنا لا نشترط في المكلف به أن يكون متعلق القدرة بالفعل، بل بالإمكان كإيمان الكافر دون خلق الأجسام. معه . وعن الثالث بمنع تماثل القدرتين .
And the first was answered by what preceded. And as for the second: [we say] that we do not stipulate in the obligated [person] that the object of capacity be [connected] to the actual [act], but rather [possibility] like the faith of the disbeliever without the creation of bodies. With it. And as for the third: by preventing the similarity of the two capacities.
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage provides responses to three objections. The first objection is answered by referencing a previous discussion. The second objection is answered by clarifying that the object of capacity (al-mukallaf bihi) does not have to be something that is actually possible to perform at the moment of obligation, but rather it suffices that it is possible in principle, like the faith of a disbeliever (which is possible even though he currently lacks capacity due to his disbelief) without needing the creation of bodies (which is impossible for humans). The third objection is answered by denying the similarity of the two capacities (divine and human).

Key Terms
الاستطاعة
القدرة
المكلف به
الإمكان
تماثل القدرتين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Obligation requires actual capacity at the moment of command.
Correction: The passage states that only possibility is required, not actuality.

Checkpoint

A) By stipulating actual capacity
B) By stipulating possibility, not actuality
C) By denying divine capacity
D) By asserting similarity of capacities

Key Takeaway
Three objections to capacity are answered: the first by prior discussion, the second by requiring only possibility, and the third by denying similarity of capacities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you often make excuses for not obeying Allah because you feel incapable, when in fact the obligation only requires possibility? How can you apply the lesson that possibility suffices to remove your excuses?
?
This lesson denies that divine and human capacities are similar. How does this affect the way you rely on your own strength versus relying on Allah's power?
?
The disbeliever's faith is possible in itself even though he currently lacks capacity. What does this teach you about the mercy of Allah in holding people accountable only for what is possible? How does this encourage you to never give up on someone's iman?

The Ash'ari Argument on Created Capacity with the Act
Ash'ari argument that capacity is an accident which does not persist.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari proof that created capacity exists simultaneously with the act, based on the principle that accidents do not persist for two moments.

Core Idea
Created capacity is an accident; it cannot persist and must coincide with the act.

This passage presents the foundational Ash'ari argument against the Mu'tazila view that capacity precedes the act. It uses the metaphysical principle that accidents do not persist for two moments, and it addresses key objections. Understanding this argument clarifies a central point of contention in Islamic theology regarding human agency and divine power.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the English translation. Note the debate between Ash'aris and Mu'tazila.
2
Identify the main proof
The proof: capacity is an accident (ʿaraḍ); accidents do not persist for two moments. Therefore, if capacity were before the act, it would vanish at the act, leading to absurdity.
Notice the logical form: if P then Q, Q leads to impossibility, so not P.
3
Examine the objection and counter-response
The objection about eternal power is dismissed. The counter-response about renewal of similars (knowledge, inclination) is examined and critiqued.
The author shows that the renewal argument either falls back to the vanishing power (same problem) or leads to the desired position.
4
Reflect on the final remark
The argument is binding on those who affirm that created power has an effect. Consider what this says about the Ash'ari stance on causality.
This shows the argument is dialectical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اختلفوا في أن الاستطاعة أي القدرة الحادثة على الفعل تكون قبله، أو فذهبت الأشاعرة وغيرهم من أهل السنة إلى أنها مع الفعل لا قبله، وأكثر المعتزلة إلى أنها قبل الفعل ثم اختلفوا في أنه هل يجب بقاؤها إلى حالة المقدور، لنا وجوه (۱) : الأول : أن القدرة عرض والعرض لا يبقى زمانين، فلو كانت قبل الفعل لانعدمت حال الفعل فيلزم وجود المقدور بدون المقدرة والمعلول بدون العلة ، وهو محال ولا يرد النقض بالقدرة القديمة لأنها ليست من قبيل الأعراض، وأجيب بعد تسليم امتناع بقاء العرض بأن المحال هو وجود المعلول بدون أن يكون له علة أصلا، واللازم هو وجوده بدون مقارنة العلة بل مع وإستحالة ذلك نفس المتنازع، ولو سلم فيجوز أن تنعدم القدرة ويحدث مثلها فيكون لها بقاء بتجدد الأمثال على الاستمرار في حال الفعل كما هو شأن العلم والميل والتمني، ونحو ذلك مما لا نزاع في جواز سبقها على متعلقاتها . وفيه نظر لأن وجود المقدور حينئذ إما بالقدرة الزائلة فيعود المحذور، أو الحاصلة وهو المطلوب. ثم لا يخفى أن الكلام إلزامي على من يقول بتأثير القدرة الحادثة .
They differed concerning capacity (al-istita'a), i.e., the created power for the act: is it before the act or not? The Ash'aris and others of Ahl al-Sunna held that it is with the act, not before it. Most Mu'tazila held that it is before the act; then they differed as to whether it must remain up to the state of the object of power. We have proofs: First: that power is an accident, and the accident does not persist for two moments. So if it were before the act, it would become nonexistent at the time of the act, necessitating the existence of the object of power without the power, and the effect without the cause, which is impossible. And the objection by the eternal power (divine attribute) is not valid because it is not of the category of accidents. And it was answered, after conceding the impossibility of the persistence of the accident, that the impossible is the existence of the effect without having any cause at all, but what is entailed is existence without concurrence with the cause, but with [something else]; and the impossibility of that is the very point of dispute. And if it is conceded, then it is possible that the power becomes nonexistent and its like comes into existence, so it has persistence by the renewal of similars continuously at the time of the act, as is the case with knowledge, inclination, wishing, and the like, concerning which there is no dispute about the permissibility of their preceding their objects. But there is a consideration [against this]: because the existence of the object of power then is either by the disappearing power, in which case the objection returns, or by the occurring power, which is the desired [position]. Then it is not hidden that the discussion is binding upon those who affirm the effect of the created power.
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage introduces the debate on whether created capacity (al-qudra al-haditha) for an act exists before the act or with it. The Ash'aris hold that it is with the act, while most Mu'tazila hold it is before. The author then presents the first proof for the Ash'ari position: capacity is an accident, and accidents do not persist for two moments. If capacity preceded the act, it would be nonexistent when the act occurs, leading to the absurdity of the act existing without a power (capable agent). The objection using divine power is dismissed because divine power is not an accident. A counter-answer suggests that if accidents cannot persist, perhaps the accident disappears and a similar one arises, providing continuity, like knowledge. However, this reply is criticized: the object of power would then be either by the vanishing power (problem) or the new one, which is the desired conclusion. The passage ends with a remark that this argument is binding on those who affirm that created power has an effect.

Key Terms
الاستطاعة
القدرة الحادثة
العرض
بقاء
العلة
المعلول
تجدد الأمثال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'ari argument is weak because it ignores that accidents could be renewed.
Correction: The passage explicitly addresses the renewal argument and shows it does not undermine the conclusion; either the renewal leaves the same problem or yields the Ash'ari position.

Checkpoint
What is the first Ash'ari argument against capacity preceding the act?
A) Capacity is a substance that cannot persist
B) Capacity is an accident that does not persist, so if before the act it would be absent at the act
C) The act would be impossible without capacity
D) Divine power is similar to human power

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari first proof for capacity with the act is based on the non-persistence of accidents, and despite objections, it stands as a decisive argument against those who hold that capacity precedes the act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your capacity for every action is created at the very moment you act, then you never possess an independent ability to act apart from Allah's creation of it. How does this realization affect your sense of self-reliance and your daily dependence on Allah?
?
The proof argues that a created power cannot persist; it must be continuously renewed. Your very ability to read this text right now is a newly created accident. Does this humbling truth change how you thank Allah for the simplest actions?
?
Consider a moment when you felt proud of your own strength or intelligence. According to this lesson, that strength was an accident created by Allah at that moment. How can this knowledge transform your view of your own achievements into moments of gratitude and humility?

Second and Third Ash'ari Arguments with Joint Reply
Two more Ash'ari arguments that capacity must accompany the act, with joint answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second and third Ash'ari arguments for capacity accompanying the act and the joint reply that distinguishes between descriptive impossibility and real possibility.

Core Idea
Impossibility in one state does not negate the power to bring about an act in another.

In this passage, al-Sanusi presents two more Ash'ari arguments that assert capacity must accompany the act, not precede it. These arguments rely on the concept of impossibility in the state of nonexistence and a logical contradiction (khulf). He then provides a joint answer that clarifies the nuance of 'impossibility' and illustrates it with the example of Zayd's standing while sitting.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: two arguments followed by a joint answer.
The arguments are marked 'second' and 'third'.
2
Identify the second argument
The second argument claims the act in its state of nonexistence is impossible, and what is impossible cannot be an object of power. Consider why this argument appeals to consensus.
3
Identify the third argument
The third argument uses a reductio (khulf): if capacity were before the act, the act would be possible before occurrence, but that leads to contradiction because capacity would then be with the act, not before.
Note that the two arguments are said to be close in meaning.
4
Work through the joint reply and analogy
The answer distinguishes two senses of impossibility: impossibility under the description of being nonexistent (not conceded) and impossibility in the time of nonexistence (also not conceded, since existence can replace nonexistence). The Zayd analogy illustrates this: standing is impossible while sitting, but possible in the time of sitting by removing sitting.
This reply shows that the act remains an object of power despite its current nonexistence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : أن الفعل حال عدمه ممتنع لاستحالة اجتماع الوجود والعدم ولا شيء من الممتنع بمقدور وفاقاً. الثالث : لو كانت القدرة قبل الفعل لكان الفعل قبل وقوعه ممكناً لكنه محال لأنه يلزم من فرض وقوعه كون القدرة معه لا قبله هذا خلف . والوجهان متقاربان وجوابهما بعد النقض بالقدرة القديمة إنه إن أريد بامتناع الفعل حال العدم وقيل الحدوث امتناعه مع وصف كونه معدومة وغير واقع فممنوع لكنه لا ينافي المقدورية فإمكان الحصول من القادر وإن أريد امتناعه في زمان عدمه وكونه غير واقع فممنوع بل هو ممكن بأن يحصل بدل عدمه الوجود كما هو شأن سائر الممكنات وهذا كقيام زيد فإنه ممتنع مع القعود وبشرطه لكنه ممكن حال القعود، وفي زمانه بأن يزول القعود ويحصل القيام .
Second: that the act in the state of its nonexistence is impossible due to the impossibility of the conjunction of existence and nonexistence, and nothing impossible is an object of power by agreement. Third: if capacity were before the act, then the act would be possible before its occurrence, but it is impossible because from the supposition of its occurrence, it follows that capacity would be with it, not before it — this is a contradiction (khulf). And the two approaches are close, and their answer after the objection by the eternal power is that if by the impossibility of the act in the state of nonexistence — and it is said: in the state of origination — is meant its impossibility with the description of being nonexistent and not occurring, then it is prevented [i.e., not conceded], but it does not negate being an object of power, so the possibility of obtaining from the capable [agent]. And if by its impossibility in the time of its nonexistence and its being not occurring is meant, then it is prevented [not conceded]; rather it is possible by bringing existence in place of its nonexistence, as is the case with all possibles. And this is like the standing of Zayd: it is impossible with the sitting and its condition, but it is possible in the state of sitting, and in its time, by the removal of sitting and the occurrence of standing.
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage presents the second and third Ash'ari arguments and their joint answer. The second argument states that the act in its state of nonexistence is impossible, and what is impossible cannot be an object of power by consensus. The third argument argues that if capacity preceded the act, the act would be possible before its occurrence, but that leads to a contradiction (khulf) because assuming its occurrence would imply capacity with it, not before. The two arguments are similar. The joint answer, after noting the objection from divine power, explains that the impossibility of the act in the state of nonexistence is ambiguous. If it means impossibility under the description of being nonexistent and not occurring, it is not conceded that this negates being an object of power, because the act can still be possible for the capable agent. If it means impossibility in the time of its nonexistence, that is also not conceded; rather, it is possible to bring about existence in place of nonexistence, like any contingent thing. An analogy is given: standing is impossible while sitting, but it is possible in the time of sitting by removing sitting and acquiring standing.

Key Terms
الممتنع
المقدور
الخلف
الوجود
العدم
الممكنات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that impossibility of the act in its state of nonexistence means the act cannot be an object of power at all.
Correction: The joint reply explains that impossibility under the description of being nonexistent does not negate being an object of power; the act is still possible for the capable agent, just as standing is possible while sitting by removing the sitting.

Checkpoint
What example is given to illustrate that something impossible under one condition can be possible in another?
A) The creation of bodies
B) The standing of Zayd while sitting
C) The faith of a disbeliever
D) The persistence of accidents

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of an act in a given state (e.g., nonexistence) does not mean it is impossible for the agent to bring it about, as contingents can replace nonexistence with existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often feel powerless to change sinful habits because they seem impossible to overcome while you are still indulging in them. If the lesson shows that impossibility in one state does not negate power to act in another, what is the 'sitting' condition you must remove to 'stand' in obedience? Will you identify one specific sin and the exact first step to break its condition this week?
?
Consider a good deed you keep postponing—prayer, charity, or reconciling with someone. You tell yourself it's impossible now because of your current circumstances. But the Zayd analogy teaches that standing is possible even while sitting, by removing the sitting. What circumstance are you using as an excuse, and how can you replace it with its opposite starting today?
?
The Ash'ari argue that capacity must accompany the act, but the joint reply insists that the act's nonexistence at one time does not make it impossible for you to perform it later. Is there an act of worship you have convinced yourself you cannot do because you haven't done it before? What does this lesson demand you to believe about your own capability with Allah's help, and what is the first action you will take right now?

Mu'tazila Arguments for Capacity Preceding Act
Mu'tazila argue capacity must precede act via two absurdities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two absurdities the Mu'tazila claim follow from the Ash'ari position that capacity accompanies the act, and why they argue capacity must precede the act.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila use two absurdities to prove capacity must precede the act.

The Mu'tazila challenge the Ash'ari view that capacity (qudra) only relates to the act at the moment of its occurrence. They present two arguments, each showing that the alternative leads to a logical or theological absurdity. The first argument concerns the meaning of bringing into existence, the second concerns the nature of obligation. This passage presents these two proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the structure: the Mu'tazila present a premise, then list two absurdities that follow.
The two absurdities are enumerated as 'الأول' and 'الثاني'.
2
Understand the first absurdity
The first absurdity is 'إيجاد الموجود وتحصيل الحاصل' — bringing into existence what already exists. Think: how does this relate to the timing of capacity?
The argument claims that if capacity only exists with the act, then it is causing something that already exists (the act is already occurring).
3
Understand the second absurdity
The second absurdity is about obligation (taklīf). The Mu'tazila argue that if the act is not within capacity before it occurs, then all obligations would be obligations of the impossible (taklīf mā lā yuṭāq), which is false by consensus.
Obligation requires the act to be possible before it is commanded; otherwise the command is meaningless.
4
Reflect on the Mu'tazila position
Consider why these two absurdities are convincing to the Mu'tazila. How do they support the view that capacity must precede the act?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتجت المعتزلة بوجوه : الأول : أن القدرة لو لم تتعلق بالفعل إلا حال وجوده وحدوثه لزم محالات : الأول : إيجاد الموجود وتحصيل الحاصل لأن هذا معنى تعلق القدرة . الثاني : بطلان التكليف لأن التكليف بالفعل إنما يكون قبل حصوله ضرورة أنه لا معنى لطلب حصول الحاصل فإذا كان الفعل قبل الوقوع غير مقدور كان جميع التكاليف الواقع تكليف ما لا يطاق، وهو باطل بالاتفاق لأن القائل بجوازه لم يقل بوقوعه فضلا عن عمومه .
And the Mu'tazila argued with proofs: First: that if capacity relates to the act only at the time of its existence and occurrence, then absurdities follow: First: bringing into existence that which already exists and obtaining the obtained, for that is the meaning of the relation of capacity. Second: the nullification of obligation (taklīf), because obligation concerning the act is only before its occurrence, necessarily because there is no meaning in seeking the occurrence of the already-occurred. So if the act before its occurrence is not an object of power, then all obligations that occur would be obligation of what is impossible (taklīf mā lā yuṭāq), and that is false by consensus, because even the one who permits it does not say it occurs, let alone its universality.
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage presents two arguments from the Mu'tazila against the Ash'ari position that capacity accompanies the act. The first argument states that if capacity (qudra) is only related to the act at the moment of its existence and occurrence, then it would entail absurdities, such as bringing into existence what already exists and obtaining what is already obtained, which is meaningless. The second argument concerns obligation (taklīf): obligation is commanded before the act occurs; if the act is not within capacity before its occurrence, then all actual obligations would be obligations of the impossible (taklīf mā lā yuṭāq), which is false by consensus. Even the one who permits the obligation of the impossible does not claim it occurs universally. These arguments support the Mu'tazila position that capacity must precede the act.

Key Terms
المعتزلة
تعلق القدرة
إيجاد الموجود
تحصيل الحاصل
التكليف
تكليف ما لا يطاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the Mu'tazila argue capacity must precede the act because the act would be impossible otherwise.
Correction: The Mu'tazila actually argue that the alternative (capacity simultaneous with act) leads to absurdities such as causing the already-existent and obligating the impossible.

Checkpoint

A) The act would be impossible
B) All obligations would be obligations of the impossible
C) Capacity would be eternal
D) The act would be created by God alone

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila use two absurdities—causing the already-existent and obligating the impossible—to argue that capacity must precede the act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a difficult command from Allah, do you ever think it is beyond your capacity? How does the Mu'tazila's argument that obligating the impossible is invalid challenge that assumption? What does that say about your own excuses?
?
The Mu'tazila say that if capacity were only at the moment of the act, then every command before that would be a demand for the impossible. Now think: Are there commands you delay obeying because you feel you need a special capacity first? What would it look like to trust that Allah has already given you the capacity to obey right now?
?
The second absurdity says that obligating the impossible nullifies obligation itself. Have you ever treated a command of Allah as if it were impossible for you, thereby effectively nullifying your own obligation? How can you change that mindset today?

Abu Hashim's Four Views on the Two Powers
Enumerates Abu Hashim's four positions on the scope of the two powers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four different views Abu Hashim proposed regarding the scope of the power residing in the heart and the power residing in the limbs.

Core Idea
Abu Hashim explored four logical possibilities for how internal and external powers relate to acts.

Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i, a prominent Mu'tazili theologian, did not settle on a single theory of how the power in the heart and the power in the limbs relate to their respective acts. Instead, he presented four distinct positions, each reflecting a different logical possibility. This lesson will walk you through each view and explain the nuance in his thinking.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the English translation to get a general sense of the four views.
Notice the repetition of 'and sometimes' (وتارة) marking each new view.
2
Identify each of the four views
List the four views in order. For each view, note which acts the heart-power and limb-power relate to.
The first view restricts each power to its own locus; the second allows universal relation but only effect on own locus; the third restricts each to all acts of its own locus; the fourth gives heart-power universal relation but limb-power limited to its own locus.
3
Understand the author's explanation of the last two views
The author explains that the matn refers to the last two views as 'specifying the two rulings with respect to the heart'. Understand what 'the two rulings' means here.
The two rulings are: (1) restriction to acts of own locus, and (2) relation to all acts of own locus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتردد أبو هاشم فزعم تارة أن كلاً من القدرة القائمة بالقلب، والقدرة القائمة بالجوارح تتعلق بأفعال محلها دون محل الأخرى، بمعنى أن القائمة بالقلب تتعلق بالإرادات والاعتقادات مثلاً دون الحركات والاعتمادات والقائمة بالجوارح على العكس.

وتارة أن كلا منهما يتعلق بالجميع إلا أنها لا تؤثر إلا في أفعال محله مثلاً: القائمة بالقلب تتعلق بأفعال القلوب والجوارح لكن يمتنع اتحاد أفعال الجوارح بها لفقد الشرائط والقائمة بالجوارح بالعكس.

وتارة أن القائمة بالقلب تتعلق بجميع أفعال القلب، والقائمة بالجوارح لا تتعلق بجميع أفعال الجوارح.

وتارة أن القائمة بالقلب تتعلق بأفعال القلوب والجوارح جميعاً وإن لم تؤثر في أفعال الجوارح، والقائمة بالجوارح لا تتعلق بأفعال القلب، وإلى القولين الأخيرين أشار في المتن بقوله وتارة خص الحكمين بالقلبية، وأراد بالحكمين أفعال محله خاصة والتعلق بجميع أفعال محله، ومحل الأخرى التعلق بجميع أفعال محله.
And Abu Hashim vacillated, sometimes claiming that each of the power subsisting in the heart and the power subsisting in the limbs relates to the acts of its locus [mahall] and not the other, meaning that the one subsisting in the heart relates to wills and beliefs, for example, and not to movements and depressions, and the one subsisting in the limbs is the opposite.

And sometimes that both relate to everything, but they only effect the acts of their locus: for example, the one subsisting in the heart relates to acts of the heart and limbs, but unification of the acts of the limbs with it is impossible due to lack of conditions, and the one subsisting in the limbs is the opposite.

And sometimes that the one subsisting in the heart relates to all acts of the heart, while the one subsisting in the limbs relates to all acts of the limbs.

And sometimes that the one subsisting in the heart relates to all acts of the heart and limbs together, even if it does not effect the acts of the limbs, and the one subsisting in the limbs does not relate to the acts of the heart. And the author [al-matan] referred to the last two opinions in his saying: 'and sometimes he specified the two rulings with respect to the heart', meaning by 'the two rulings' the acts of its own locus specifically, and the relation to all acts of its locus, and the locus of the other is the relation to all acts of its locus.
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This passage enumerates the four positions (aqwal) of Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i on the scope of the two powers: the power (qudra) residing in the heart (qalbiyya) and that residing in the limbs (jawarih). Each position considers whether these powers relate only to the acts of their own locus, to all acts (both heart and limb) with different conditions, or with full scope. The passage categorizes the first two as general theories, then the last two as specified in the text (al-matan). The author explains that by 'the two rulings' (al-hukmayn) the text means: (1) restriction to acts of one's own locus, and (2) relation to all acts of one's own locus. This enumeration shows Abu Hashim's uncertainty and the complexities of relating internal and external agency.

Key Terms
القدرة القائمة بالقلب
القدرة القائمة بالجوارح
أفعال المحل
القلبية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Abu Hashim held a single definitive view on the scope of the two powers.
Correction: He actually proposed four different positions without settling on one, showing his uncertainty on the matter.

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hashim's first view, what does the power in the heart relate to?
A) Only acts of the heart (e.g., wills and beliefs).
B) Only acts of the limbs (e.g., movements).
C) Both heart and limb acts equally.
D) Neither.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hashim's four views illustrate the complexity of relating internal and external agency in kalam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Abu Hashim's views introduce a gap between what your heart wills and what your limbs do. When you perform an act of worship mechanically, is your heart-power relating to that act but not effecting it? What inner condition would you need to change so that both powers fully cohere?
?
Consider the fourth view: the heart relates to limb-acts but does not effect them. Are there actions you perform daily that your heart is not fully present in? How real is your faith if the power of your heart is disconnected from the motions of your body?
?
If the heart-power can relate to all acts of the limbs, then your accountability before Allah encompasses not just your intentions but also the alignment of your outward actions with your inner state. Which of these four views most accurately describes your current life, and what one step will you take tonight to bring your heart and limbs into harmony?
